<span id="page-41-6"></span>(d) Overwrite the values of  $\mathbf{w}.\mathbf{W}.(z',\mathbf{z}'_{\mathcal{H}},\mathbf{r}'_{\mathcal{H}})$  and the value of  $\mathbf{inst.x.h}'_{\mathcal{H}}$  as follows:

$$\begin{aligned} & \text{w.}\mathbf{W}.z' \leftarrow z_{i+k} \\ & \text{w.}\mathbf{W}.\mathbf{z}' \leftarrow \mathsf{SSS.share}(\mathbb{F}, z_{i+k}, \mathbf{z}'_{\mathcal{C}}, \mathcal{C}, t) \\ & \text{w.}\mathbf{W}.\mathbf{r}'_{\mathcal{H}} \overset{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}^{|\mathcal{H}|} \\ & \text{inst.x.} h'_{j} \leftarrow \mathsf{HCom.Commit}(z'_{j}; r'_{j}), \quad \forall j \in \mathcal{H} \end{aligned}$$

<span id="page-41-4"></span>(e) Overwrite  $\mathbf{w}.(\mathbf{E}, r_{\mathbf{E}}, r_{\mathbf{W}})$  as follows:

$$\begin{split} \mathbf{Z} &= (\mathsf{inst}, \mathsf{w}).(\mathbf{W}, \mathsf{x}, u) \\ \mathsf{w}.\mathbf{E} &\leftarrow (\mathbf{A} \cdot \mathbf{Z}) \circ (\mathbf{B} \cdot \mathbf{Z}) - \mathsf{inst}.u \cdot (\mathbf{C} \cdot \mathbf{Z}) \\ \mathsf{w}.r_{\mathbf{W}} &\leftarrow \mathsf{Ped}.\mathsf{TOpen}(\tau_W, \mathsf{w}^{\mathrm{Old}}.\mathbf{W}, \mathsf{w}.\mathbf{W}, \mathsf{w}^{\mathrm{Old}}.r_{\mathbf{W}}) \\ \mathsf{w}.r_{\mathbf{E}} &\leftarrow \mathsf{Ped}.\mathsf{TOpen}(\tau_E, \mathsf{w}^{\mathrm{Old}}.\mathbf{E}, \mathsf{w}.\mathbf{E}, \mathsf{w}^{\mathrm{Old}}.r_{\mathbf{E}}) \end{split}$$

where  $w^{\mathrm{Old}}$  is the value of w at the end of step 3b of  $\mathcal{S}$ , before it was modified.

- (f) Complete the simulation of Increment by executing steps 10 11.
- 4. Simulate steps 6 7 of  $\mathcal{P}(\mathsf{pk}, i, k, z_0, z_i, \Pi_i, W)$ . As before,  $\mathcal{S}$  performs the steps assigned to  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{CFS}}$  and the honest provers, including any interactions with the internal simulation of  $\mathcal{A}$ . At the end of step 7,  $\mathcal{P}$  has computed  $(z_{i+k}, \Pi_{i+k})$ .
- <span id="page-41-0"></span>5. Output whatever final message  $\mathcal{A}$  outputs.

The following claim will be useful later on.

<span id="page-41-8"></span>Claim 26. The value of (inst, w) computed in steps 3a - 3e of S satisfy (inst, w)  $\in \mathcal{R}_F$ .

*Proof.* inst has components  $(\overline{E}, u, \overline{W}, x)$ , and w has components  $(\mathbf{E}, r_{\mathbf{E}}, \mathbf{W}, r_{\mathbf{W}})$ . First, step 3e of S chooses  $\mathbf{E}$  to satisfy:

$$(\mathbf{A} \cdot \mathbf{Z}) \circ (\mathbf{B} \cdot \mathbf{Z}) = u \cdot (\mathbf{C} \cdot \mathbf{Z}) + \mathbf{E}$$

where  $\mathbf{Z} = (\mathbf{W}, \mathsf{x}, u)$ .

Second, step 3e of S chooses  $r_{\mathbf{W}}, r_{\mathbf{E}}$  using Ped.TOpen. This function chooses the unique value of w. $r_{\mathbf{W}}$  that satisfies:

$$\begin{aligned} \mathsf{Ped.Commit}(\mathsf{pp}_W, \mathsf{w}.\mathbf{W}; \mathsf{w}.r_{\mathbf{W}}) &= \mathsf{Ped.Commit}(\mathsf{pp}_W, \mathsf{w}^{\mathrm{Old}}.\mathbf{W}; \mathsf{w}^{\mathrm{Old}}.r_{\mathbf{W}}) \\ &= \mathsf{inst.}\overline{W} \end{aligned}$$

<span id="page-41-1"></span><sup>&</sup>lt;sup>2</sup>Since  $\mathcal{A}$  may deviate from the protocol,  $(\omega_{\ell}^{\prime j})_{j \in \mathcal{C}}$  may be different from the original values  $(\omega_{\ell}^{j})_{j \in \mathcal{C}}$  given as input to  $\mathcal{A}$ .

{42}------------------------------------------------

Likewise, Ped.TOpen also chooses the unique value of w.r<sup>E</sup> that satisfies:

$$\mathsf{inst}.\overline{E} = \mathsf{Ped}.\mathsf{Commit}(\mathsf{pp}_E, \mathsf{w}.\mathbf{E}; \mathsf{w}.r_\mathbf{E})$$

This shows that all the conditions of R<sup>F</sup> [\(Eq. \(3.2\)\)](#page-14-2) are satisfied. Therefore, (inst,w) ∈ R<sup>F</sup> .

Now we argue that S with access to Fzk correctly simulates the real-world protocol πzk.

First, in πzk, the corrupted provers C ⊆ [n] receive pp from fG. In the ideal world, S samples pp using the same procedure as fG, except that S uses Ped.TGen in place of Ped.Gen to sample pp<sup>E</sup> and pp<sup>W</sup> . Both functions sample (ppE, pp<sup>W</sup> ) from the same distribution; the only difference is that Ped.TGen also outputs trapdoors. In summary, the distribution of pp is the same in the real and ideal worlds. Additionally, S sends pp to A.

Second, in πzk, the provers locally compute (pk, vk) ← K(pp, F) and b ← V(vk, i, z0, z<sup>i</sup> , Πi), which do not entail sending messages among the parties. S simulates this in step [1c](#page-40-1) by following the real-world procedure.

Third, in πzk, the provers compute (zi+k, Πi+k) ← P(pk, i, k, z0, z<sup>i</sup> , Π<sup>i</sup> , W). Let us argue that A's view during P is correctly simulated. It suffices to consider the case where (1) 1 = b = V(vk, i, z0, z<sup>i</sup> , Πi), and (2) k ≥ 1 because otherwise, A does not receive any messages during the real-world P or during S's simulation of P.

S simulates P mainly by following the steps of P faithfully, playing the role of the honest provers and FCFS, and interacting with its internal simulation of A. The only difference between the real-world execution of P and S's simulation of P is that in the final execution of Increment, S modifies the values of w and inst.x. We will show that none of these modifications affect the view of A during P.

Claim 27. S correctly simulates A's view during steps [1](#page-31-0) - [6](#page-31-7) of P (pk, i, k, z0, z<sup>i</sup> , Π<sup>i</sup> , W).

<span id="page-42-1"></span>Proof. During steps [1](#page-31-0) - [6](#page-31-7) of P, A receives the following messages, and S correctly simulates them:

1. For each ℓ ∈ {i, . . . , i + k − 1}, when Fold [\(Fig. 4.6\)](#page-27-0) is called in step [5a,](#page-31-5) A receives π<sup>ℓ</sup> and Instℓ+1. π<sup>ℓ</sup> is computed by NIFS.P<sup>1</sup> [\(Section 3.5.1\)](#page-16-0) as follows:

$$\pi_\ell = \mathsf{Ped.Commit}(\mathsf{pp}_E, \mathbf{T}; r_{\mathbf{T}})$$

T are the folding cross terms, which depend on (Instℓ, Wℓ),(instℓ,wℓ), and may have different distributions in the real and ideal worlds. However, r<sup>T</sup> is uniformly random. Then π<sup>ℓ</sup> is identically distributed in the real and ideal worlds because the Pedersen commitment scheme is perfectly hiding.

<span id="page-42-2"></span>2. Additionally, for each ℓ ∈ {i, . . . , i +k −1}, Instℓ+1 (computed by Fold in step [5a](#page-31-5) of P) has the same distribution in the real and ideal worlds. In both worlds Instℓ+1 is computed by NIFS.P<sup>2</sup> according to [Eq. \(3.4\),](#page-16-1) which is a deterministic function of (Instℓ, instℓ, T , r).

We will show that all of the inputs (Instℓ, instℓ, T , r) are part of A's existing view, so S uses the correctly distributed inputs. First, Inst<sup>ℓ</sup> is either contained in Π<sup>i</sup> (when ℓ = i) or given to A by Fold in step [5a](#page-31-5) of P. Second, inst<sup>ℓ</sup> is either contained in Π<sup>i</sup> (when ℓ = i) or given to A by Increment in step [5b](#page-31-6) of P. Either way, (Instℓ, instℓ) are part of A's view. Third, T is the folding proof π ′ that A chooses during Fold. Fourth, r is computed as follows:

$$r = \mathsf{RO}(\mathsf{vk}_{\mathsf{NIFS}}, \mathsf{Inst}_\ell, \mathsf{inst}_\ell, \overline{T})$$

(RO, vkNIFS, Instℓ, instℓ, T) are all determined by A's view, so r is as well.

Finally, we've shown that Instℓ+1 is a deterministic function of values that are part of A's view. In the ideal world, S computes Instℓ+1 from the correctly distributed inputs according to the same procedure as the real-world function Fold. Therefore Instℓ+1 has the same distribution in the real and ideal worlds.

<span id="page-42-0"></span>3. For each ℓ ∈ {i, . . . , i + k − 2}, when Increment [\(Fig. 4.7\)](#page-28-0) is called in step [5b,](#page-31-6) A receives instℓ+1. First, instℓ+1 comprises (E, u, W , x). (E, u) are computed from public constants as follows:

$$\begin{split} (\mathbf{E}, r_{\mathbf{E}}, u) &= (\mathbf{0^M}, 0, 1) \\ \overline{E} &= \mathsf{Ped.Commit}(\mathsf{pp}_E, \mathbf{E}; r_{\mathbf{E}}) \end{split}$$

{43}------------------------------------------------

Therefore,  $(\overline{E}, u)$  are identically distributed in the real and ideal worlds.

Second,  $\overline{W}$  is computed as:

$$\overline{W} = \mathsf{Ped.Commit}(\mathsf{pp}_W, \mathbf{W}; r_{\mathbf{W}})$$

Between the real and ideal worlds, **W** may have different distributions, but  $r_{\mathbf{W}}$  is uniformly random in both worlds. Then  $\overline{W}$  has the same distribution in both worlds because the Pedersen commitment is perfectly hiding.

Third, x is the output  $\mathbf{h}'$  of  $H_F$ , which is computed by the following procedure:

$$\begin{split} \mathsf{Inst}' &= \begin{cases} \mathsf{inst}_\perp, & \ell = 0 \\ \mathsf{NIFS}.\mathcal{V}(\mathsf{vk}_{\mathsf{NIFS}}, \mathsf{Inst}_\ell, \mathsf{inst}_\ell, \pi_\ell) & \mathsf{else} \end{cases} \\ \mathsf{Inst}'' &= \mathsf{Inst}' \\ h_0' &= \mathsf{HCom.Commit}(\mathsf{vk}_{\mathsf{NIFS}}, \mathsf{increment}, \ell + 1, z_0, \mathsf{inst}_\perp, \mathsf{Inst}''; 0) \\ h_j' &= \mathsf{HCom.Commit}(z_j'; r_j'), \quad \forall j \in [n] \\ \mathsf{x} &= \mathbf{h}' = (h_j')_{j \in \{0\} \cup [n]} \end{split}$$

Note that  $h'_0$  is computed as a deterministic function of  $(\mathsf{vk}_{\mathsf{NIFS}}, \ell, z_0, \mathsf{inst}_{\perp}, \mathsf{Inst}_{\ell}, \mathsf{inst}_{\ell}, \pi_{\ell})$  and  $\mathsf{RO}$ . These values are already known to  $\mathcal{A}$ , so  $\mathcal{S}$  correctly simulates the computation of  $h'_0$ .

For each corrupted prover  $j \in \mathcal{C}$ ,  $h'_j$  is computed from  $(z'_j, r'_j)$  and RO.  $(z'_j, r'_j)$  are supplied by  $\mathcal{A}$  as inputs to Increment, so the value of  $h'_j$  is determined by  $\mathcal{A}$ 's existing view, and  $\mathcal{S}$  correctly simulates  $h'_j$ .

For each honest prover  $j \in \mathcal{H}$ ,  $z'_j$  may have different distributions in the real and ideal worlds. However, real- and ideal-world distributions of  $h'_j$  are indistinguishable because  $r'_j$  is sampled by Increment, and because HCom is hiding.

In summary x and the rest of inst $_{\ell+1}$  are correctly simulated by  $\mathcal{S}$ .

4. When  $\ell = i + k - 1$ , and Increment is called in step 5b of  $\mathcal{P}$ ,  $\mathcal{A}$  receives inst<sub>i+k</sub>.

S simulates  $inst_{i+k}$  in step 3, where  $inst_{i+k}$  is referred to as inst. In step 3b, S computes an initial value of  $inst_{i+k}$  by following Increment honestly. This simulated value of  $inst_{i+k}$  has the same distribution as the real-world value. The proof of this fact is the same as for the previous invocations of Increment (see item 3 above).

Next, S overwrites the value of  $\operatorname{inst}_{i+k}.x.h'_{\mathcal{H}}$ . For each  $j \in \mathcal{H}$ , S chooses a new value for  $z'_j$ , samples a fresh random value for  $r'_j$ , and sets:

$$inst.x.h'_i \leftarrow \mathsf{HCom.Commit}(z'_i; r'_i), \quad \forall j \in \mathcal{H}$$

By the hiding property of  $\mathsf{HCom}$ , the ideal-world distribution of  $\mathsf{inst.x.} h'_j$  is still the same as in the real world.

- 5. When Fold is called in step 6a and 6c of  $\mathcal{P}$ ,  $\mathcal{S}$  correctly simulates the view of  $\mathcal{A}$ . This follows a similar argument to the one presented in items 1 and 2.
- 6. When Rand (Fig. 4.9) is called in step 6b,  $\mathcal{A}$  receives inst'.

Rand computes inst' using inputs  $\mathbf{W}^{\text{plaintext}}$  and  $(\mathbf{W}^{j})_{j\in\mathcal{C}}$  supplied by  $\mathcal{A}$ , as well as random values sampled internally by Rand. inst' does not depend on any witnesses stored by  $\mathcal{F}_{\text{CFS}}$ , so the distribution of inst', given  $\mathcal{A}$ 's view, is the same in the real and ideal worlds.

7. When  $\mathsf{ZK}$  is called in step  $\mathsf{6d}$  of  $\mathcal{P}$ ,  $\mathcal{A}$  receives the resulting value of  $\mathsf{inst''}$ . We will show that  $\mathsf{inst''}$  is correctly simulated by  $\mathcal{S}$ . The proof of this fact follows a similar argument to the one made for Increment in item 3 because  $\mathsf{ZK}$  and Increment follow similar algorithms.

First, inst" comprises  $(\overline{E}, u, \overline{W}, x)$ . Second,  $(\overline{E}, u)$  are computed as

$$\overline{E} = \mathsf{Ped.Commit}(\mathsf{pp}_E, \mathbf{0}^M; 0)$$
 
$$u = 1$$

in the real and ideal worlds. Third,  $\overline{W} = \mathsf{Ped.Commit}(\mathsf{pp}_W, \mathbf{W}; r_{\mathbf{W}})$ .  $\overline{W}$  has the same distribution in the real and ideal worlds because  $r_{\mathbf{W}}$  is sampled uniformly at random in both worlds, and

{44}------------------------------------------------

Ped.Commit is perfectly hiding. Fourth,  $x = \mathbf{h}'$  is the output of  $H_F$ , which is computed by the following procedure:

$$\begin{split} &\mathsf{Inst}' = \mathsf{NIFS}.\mathcal{V}(\mathsf{vk_{NIFS}}, \mathsf{Inst}_{i+k}, \mathsf{inst}_{i+k}, \pi) \\ &\mathsf{Inst}'' = \mathsf{NIFS}.\mathcal{V}(\mathsf{vk_{NIFS}}, \mathsf{Inst}', \mathsf{inst}', \pi') \\ &h_0' = \mathsf{HCom}.\mathsf{Commit}(\mathsf{vk_{NIFS}}, \mathsf{zk}, i+k, z_0, \mathsf{inst}_\perp, \mathsf{Inst}''; 0) \\ &h_j' = \mathsf{HCom}.\mathsf{Commit}(z_j'; r_j'), \quad \forall j \in [n] \\ &\mathsf{x} = \mathbf{h}' = (h_j')_{j \in \{0\} \cup [n]} \end{split}$$

Note that  $h'_0$  is computed as a deterministic function of  $(\mathsf{vk}_{\mathsf{NIFS}}, i+k, z_0, \mathsf{inst}_{\perp}, \mathsf{Inst}_{i+k}, \mathsf{inst}', \pi, \pi')$  and  $\mathsf{RO}$ . These values are already known to  $\mathcal{A}$ , so  $\mathcal{S}$  correctly simulates the computation of  $h'_0$ .

For each corrupted prover  $j \in \mathcal{C}$ ,  $h'_j$  is computed from  $(z'_j, r'_j)$  and RO.  $r'_j$  is supplied by  $\mathcal{A}$  as an input to ZK, and  $z'_j = z_j$ , which was supplied by  $\mathcal{A}$  as input to the most recent invocation of Increment. Therefore, the value of  $h'_j$  is determined by  $\mathcal{A}$ 's existing view, and  $\mathcal{S}$  correctly simulates  $h'_j$ .

For each honest prover  $j \in \mathcal{H}$ , the real- and ideal-world distributions of  $h'_j$  are indistinguishable because  $r'_j$  is sampled by ZK, and because of the hiding property of HCom.

In summary x and the rest of inst" are correctly simulated by S.

In step 7 of  $\mathcal{P}$ , Output is invoked, and  $\mathcal{A}$  receives  $\Pi_{i+k} = (\mathsf{Inst''}, \mathsf{W''}, \mathsf{inst''}, \mathsf{w''})$ . We argue below that  $\mathcal{S}$  correctly simulates  $\Pi_{i+k}$ .

Claim 28. The distribution of (Inst'', W'', inst'', w'') computed in step 7 of  $\mathcal{P}$ , conditioned on  $\mathcal{A}$ 's view up to that point, is the same in the real and ideal worlds.

*Proof.* Recall that  $\Pi_{i+k} = (\mathsf{Inst}'', \mathsf{W}'', \mathsf{inst}'', \mathsf{w}'')$ . We will show that each variable is correctly simulated.

- 1. Inst" is part of  $\mathcal{A}$ 's existing view because it was chosen by  $\mathcal{A}$  during step 6c (Fold) of  $\mathcal{P}$ .
- 2. inst" is part of  $\mathcal{A}$ 's existing view because it was chosen by  $\mathcal{A}$  during step 6d (ZK) of  $\mathcal{P}$ .
- 3. w" is computed by ZK in step 6d of  $\mathcal{P}$  as follows. w" comprises  $(\mathbf{E}, r_{\mathbf{E}}, \mathbf{W}, r_{\mathbf{W}})$ . In the real and ideal worlds,  $\mathbf{E} = \mathbf{0}^M$ ,  $r_{\mathbf{E}} = 0$ , and  $r_{\mathbf{W}}$  is sampled uniformly at random. Next,  $\mathbf{W}$  comprises the wire values and inputs of the circuit:

$$H_F(\mathsf{vk}_\mathsf{NIFS},\mathsf{increment},\mathsf{zk},i+k,z_0,z,\mathbf{0},\mathsf{inst}_\perp,\mathsf{Inst}_{i+k},\mathsf{inst}_{i+k},\mathsf{inst}',\pi,\pi',\mathbf{h},\mathbf{z},\mathbf{z}',\mathbf{r},\mathbf{r}')$$

We will show that the inputs to  $H_F$  have the same distribution in the real and ideal worlds.

(a) Most inputs to  $H_F$ , specifically

$$(\mathsf{vk}_{\mathsf{NIFS}},\mathsf{increment},\mathsf{zk},i+k,z_0,\mathbf{0},\mathsf{inst}_\perp,\mathsf{Inst}_{i+k},\mathsf{inst}_{i+k},\mathsf{inst}',\pi,\pi',\mathbf{h},\mathbf{z}_\mathcal{C},\mathbf{z}'_\mathcal{C},\mathbf{r}_\mathcal{C},\mathbf{r}'_\mathcal{C}),$$

are determined by  $\mathcal{A}$ 's view so far, so they have the same distribution in the real and ideal worlds.

The only remaining inputs to  $H_F$  are  $(z, \mathbf{z}_{\mathcal{H}}, \mathbf{z}'_{\mathcal{H}}, \mathbf{r}_{\mathcal{H}}, \mathbf{r}'_{\mathcal{H}})$ .

(b)  $(z, \mathbf{z}_{\mathcal{H}}, \mathbf{r}_{\mathcal{H}})$ : In the real world, ZK sets

$$(z, \mathbf{z}_{\mathcal{H}}, \mathbf{r}_{\mathcal{H}}) \leftarrow \mathsf{w}_B.(z', \mathbf{z}'_{\mathcal{H}}, \mathbf{r}'_{\mathcal{H}})$$

<span id="page-44-0"></span>where

$$\mathbf{w}_{B}.\mathbf{z}' = z_{i+k}$$

$$\mathbf{w}_{B}.\mathbf{z}' = \mathsf{SSS.share}(\mathbb{F}, z_{i+k}, \mathbf{w}_{B}.\mathbf{z}'_{\mathcal{C}}, \mathcal{C}, t)$$

$$\mathbf{w}_{B}.\mathbf{r}'_{\mathcal{H}} \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}^{|\mathcal{H}|}$$

$$(4.9)$$

In the ideal world, step 3d of S reassigns  $w_B(z', \mathbf{z}'_{\mathcal{H}}, \mathbf{r}'_{\mathcal{H}})$  to match the real-world distribution. First, note that  $\mathbf{w}$  in step 3d of S refers to the witness  $\mathbf{w}_{i+k}$ , which is known as  $\mathbf{w}_B$  during

{45}------------------------------------------------

the execution of ZK. Second, we claim that the value of  $z_{i+k}$  returned by  $\mathcal{F}_{zk}$  in step 3c of  $\mathcal{S}$  has the same distribution as in the real world. This is because  $\mathcal{S}$  sent to  $\mathcal{F}_{zk}$  the actual witnesses  $(W'_j)_{j\in\mathcal{C}}$  that the corrupted parties used in the real-world protocol. Third, it is clear by inspection that step 3d of  $\mathcal{S}$  computes  $w_B.(z', \mathbf{z}'_{\mathcal{H}}, \mathbf{r}'_{\mathcal{H}})$  according to Eq. (4.9)

In summary, the real- and ideal-world distributions of  $(z, \mathbf{z}_{\mathcal{H}}, \mathbf{r}_{\mathcal{H}})$  are the same.

- (c)  $\mathbf{z}'_{\mathcal{H}}$ : In the real and ideal worlds,  $\mathbf{z}'_{\mathcal{H}} = \mathbf{z}_{\mathcal{H}}$ , so  $\mathbf{z}'_{\mathcal{H}}$  has the same distribution in both worlds.
- (d)  $\mathbf{r}'_{\mathcal{H}}$  is sampled uniformly at random in the real and ideal worlds.

Since the inputs to  $H_F$  have the same distribution in the real and ideal worlds, the value of **W** that is stored in w" also has the same distribution in the real and ideal worlds. This shows that w" is identically distributed in both worlds.

4. W" is computed by folding the following witnesses together:

$$\mathsf{W}_i,\mathsf{w}_i,\ldots,\mathsf{w}_{i+k},\mathsf{w}'$$

Each of these witnesses has the components  $\mathbf{W} = (\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}, \mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{shared}}, \mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{mixed}}, (\mathbf{W}^{j})_{j \in [n]})$  and  $(\mathbf{E}, r_{\mathbf{E}}, r_{\mathbf{W}})$ . Folding takes a linear combination of the witnesses, so the  $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}$  component of  $\mathbf{W}''$  is a linear combination of the  $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}$  component of all the constituent witnesses, and likewise for the other components of  $\mathbf{W}''$ .

We will show that every component of W" is either uniformly random due to the randomness of Rand, or it is completely determined by  $\mathcal{A}$ 's view. In either case,  $\mathcal{S}$  correctly simulates the distribution of that component.

(a)  $\mathbf{W}^{\text{plaintext}}$ : This component is entirely determined by  $\mathcal{A}$ 's view. First,  $(W_i, w_i)$  are known to  $\mathcal{A}$  because they are part of  $\Pi_i$ .

Second, for every  $\ell \in \{i+1,\ldots,i+k\}$ ,  $\mathbf{w}_{\ell}$  is computed by Increment, and the  $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}$  component of  $\mathbf{w}_{\ell}$  comprises all the inputs and intermediate wire values of the circuit

$$H^{\mathsf{plaintext}}\left(\mathsf{vk}_{\mathsf{NIFS}},\mathsf{mode},\mathsf{mode}',\ell-1,z_0,\mathsf{inst}_{\perp},\mathsf{Inst},\mathsf{inst},\mathsf{inst}',\pi,\pi',\mathbf{h}\right)$$

In this case,

$$(\mathsf{Inst},\mathsf{inst}',\pi,\pi') = (\mathsf{Inst}_{\ell-1},\mathsf{inst}_{\ell-1},\mathsf{inst}_{\perp},\pi_{\ell-1},0)$$
  
$$\mathbf{h} = \mathsf{inst}_{\ell-1}.\mathsf{x}$$

Note that the inputs to  $H^{\mathsf{plaintext}}$  are all part of  $\mathcal{A}$ 's view. Therefore, the  $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}$  component of  $\mathsf{w}_\ell$  is determined by  $\mathcal{A}$ 's view.

Third, the  $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}$  component of  $\mathsf{w}'$  (which is generated by Rand) is chosen by the corrupted provers. Finally, the  $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}$  component of  $\mathsf{W}''$  is a public linear combination of the  $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}$  components of  $(\mathsf{W}_i, \mathsf{w}_i, \ldots, \mathsf{w}_{i+k}, \mathsf{w}')$ , so it is entirely determined by  $\mathcal{A}$ 's view. Therefore,  $\mathcal{S}$  simulates this component of  $\mathsf{W}''$  correctly.

(b)  $[\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{shared}}, \mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{mixed}}, (\mathbf{W}^{j})_{j \in \mathcal{H}}]$ : These components of W" are uniformly random due to the randomness of Rand. In step 6b of  $\mathcal{P}$ , Rand samples the  $[\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{shared}}, \mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{mixed}}, (\mathbf{W}^{j})_{j \in \mathcal{H}}]$  components of w' uniformly at random. Furthermore, these values are independent of  $\mathcal{A}$ 's view at the start of Output.

Then step 6c of  $\mathcal{P}$  folds W' and w', which results in W" being a linear combination of W' and w'. Due to the randomness of w', the  $[\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{shared}}, \mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{mixed}}, (\mathbf{W}^{j})_{j \in \mathcal{H}}]$  components of W" will be uniformly random and independent of  $\mathcal{A}$ 's view so far.

(c)  $(\mathbf{W}^j)_{j\in\mathcal{C}}$ : These components of W" are entirely determined by  $\mathcal{A}$ 's view. First,  $(\mathbf{W}_i, \mathbf{w}_i)$  are known to  $\mathcal{A}$  because they are part of  $\Pi_i$ .

Second, for every  $\ell \in \{i+1,\ldots,i+k\}$  and every  $j \in \mathcal{C}$ ,  $\mathsf{w}_{\ell}$  is computed by Increment, and the  $\mathbf{W}^{j}$  component of  $\mathsf{w}_{\ell}$  comprises all the inputs and intermediate wire values of the circuit:

$$H^j(h_j, z_j, z_j', r_j, r_j')$$

Note that the inputs to  $H^j$  are known to  $\mathcal{A}$ .  $h_j$  is contained in  $\mathsf{inst}_{\ell-1}.\mathsf{x}$ .  $(z_j', r_j')$  are provided by  $\mathcal{A}$  as inputs to the invocation of Increment that computes  $\mathsf{w}_\ell$ , and  $(z_j, r_j)$  were either provided

{46}------------------------------------------------

by  $\mathcal{A}$  as inputs to the previous invocation of Increment or they are part of  $w_i$ . Therefore the  $\mathbf{W}^j$  component of  $w_\ell$  is determined by  $\mathcal{A}$ 's view.

Third, the  $\mathbf{W}^j$  component of  $\mathbf{w}'$  is supplied by  $\mathcal{A}$  as an input to Rand.

Finally, the  $\mathbf{W}^j$  component of W'' is a public linear combination of the  $\mathbf{W}^j$  components of  $(W_i, w_i, \dots, w_{i+k}, w')$ , so it is entirely determined by  $\mathcal{A}$ 's view. Therefore,  $\mathcal{S}$  simulates this component of W'' correctly.

(d)  $(\mathbf{E}, r_{\mathbf{E}}, r_{\mathbf{W}})$  are determined by  $\mathcal{A}$ 's view and  $\mathbf{W}$  as the unique values that make  $(\mathsf{Inst}'', \mathsf{W}'')$  accepting.

First, (Inst", W") are computed by folding together the following pairs:

<span id="page-46-4"></span>
$$(Inst_i, W_i), (inst_i, w_i), \dots, (inst_{i+k}, w_{i+k}), (inst', w')$$
 (4.10)

(In this notation, each instance refers to the value sent to  $\mathcal{A}$  during Increment, Rand, etc, and not to the value that  $\mathcal{A}$  chooses in response).

Second, each of the instance-witness pairs in Eq. (4.10) is in  $\mathcal{R}_F$ . (Inst<sub>i</sub>, W<sub>i</sub>) and (inst<sub>i</sub>, w<sub>i</sub>) are both in  $\mathcal{R}_F$  because when  $\mathcal{V}$  verifies  $\Pi_i$  in step 2 of  $\mathcal{P}$ ,  $\mathcal{V}$  only accepts if (Inst<sub>i</sub>, W<sub>i</sub>) and (inst<sub>i</sub>, w<sub>i</sub>) are in  $\mathcal{R}_F$ . Next, for each  $\ell \in \{i+1,\ldots,i+k-1\}$ , (inst<sub>\elli</sub>, w<sub>\elli</sub>)  $\in \mathcal{R}_F$  due to the correctness of Increment. Next, (inst<sub>i+k</sub>, w<sub>i+k</sub>) are simulated in step 3 of  $\mathcal{S}$ , where they are known as (inst, w). Claim 26 shows that (inst<sub>i+k</sub>, w<sub>i+k</sub>)  $\in \mathcal{R}_F$ . Finally, (inst', w')  $\in \mathcal{R}_F$  by the correctness of Rand.

Third, in the real and ideal worlds, (Inst", W") are an accepting instance-witness pair because they are computed by folding together a sequence of accepting instance-witness pairs (Eq. (4.10)). Then the following equations are satisfied:

$$\begin{split} \mathbf{Z} &= (\mathbf{W}, \mathbf{x}, u) \\ (\mathbf{A} \cdot \mathbf{Z}) \circ (\mathbf{B} \cdot \mathbf{Z}) &= u \cdot (\mathbf{C} \cdot \mathbf{Z}) + \mathbf{E} \\ \overline{E} &= \mathsf{Ped.Commit}(\mathsf{pp}_E, \mathbf{E}; r_{\mathbf{E}}) \\ \overline{W} &= \mathsf{Ped.Commit}(\mathsf{pp}_W, \mathbf{W}; r_{\mathbf{W}}) \end{split} \tag{4.11}$$

<span id="page-46-5"></span>Furthermore,  $\operatorname{Inst}'' = (\overline{E}, u, \overline{W}, \mathsf{x})$  is already part of  $\mathcal{A}$ 's view at the start of Output. Then given  $\operatorname{Inst}'' = (\overline{E}, u, \overline{W}, \mathsf{x})$  and  $\mathbf{W}$ , there is only one set of values for  $(\mathbf{E}, r_{\mathbf{E}}, r_{\mathbf{W}})$  that satisfy Eq. (4.11).

In summary, every component of W'' is determined by the adversary's view or it is uniformly random due to the randomness of Rand, so S correctly simulates W''.

This shows that the values (Inst'', W'', inst'', w'') have the same distribution in the real and ideal worlds.  $\square$ 

We have argued that S correctly simulates the corrupted parties' view of the entire protocol of  $\pi_{zk}$ . This completes the proof of t-zero-knowledge.

#### <span id="page-46-0"></span>4.5.4 Succinctness

<span id="page-46-3"></span>Lemma 29 (Succinctness). The collaborative IVC construction in Section 4.4 satisfies succinctness (Definition 12).

*Proof.* The proof  $\Pi_{i+k}$  that is output by  $\mathcal{P}$  simply comprises two instance-witness pairs in the relation  $\mathcal{R}_F$ . Each pair comprises 2 group elements  $(\overline{E}, \overline{W})$  and L + M + N + 3 field elements  $(\mathsf{x}, \mathbf{E}, \mathbf{W}, u, r_{\mathbf{E}}, r_{\mathbf{W}})$ . The size of each pair does not grow with i or k.

## <span id="page-46-1"></span>4.5.5 Communication Complexity

<span id="page-46-2"></span>Here we describe the communication complexity of our collaborative IVC prover protocol ( $\mathcal{P}$  in Section 4.4). We will analyze the version of the protocol that uses  $\pi_{\mathsf{CFS}}$  (Section 5.1) in place of  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{CFS}}$ . This protocol is defined in the ( $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{input}}$ ,  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{rand}}$ ,  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{coin}}$ ,  $\mathcal{F}_{F}$ )-hybrid model. The costs that we focus on are: (1) the number of field or group elements broadcast by each party, (2) the total number of field elements that are secret shared by  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{input}}$ , secret shared by  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{rand}}$ , or sampled by  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{coin}}$ , (3) the number of times  $\mathcal{F}_{F}$  is called.

{47}------------------------------------------------

Lemma 30. For any number of provers n, any well-formed (pk, vk), any i, k ∈ {0} ∪ N, and any z0, z<sup>i</sup> , Π<sup>i</sup> , W for which V(vk, i, z0, z<sup>i</sup> , Πi) = 1, P (pk, i, k, z0, z<sup>i</sup> , Π<sup>i</sup> , W) has the following communication costs:

- 1. 3k + 2M + 2N + 10 field or group elements are broadcast by each party.
- 2. At most 4M + 6N + n + 10 field elements are secret shared by Finput, secret shared by Frand, or sampled by Fcoin.
- 3. k calls to F<sup>F</sup> are made.

Proof. We obtain the following statistics from inspecting the protocol:

- P calls πInput 2 times, πFold k + 2 times, πIncrement k times, πZK 1 time, πRand 1 time, and πOutput 1 time to output 2 instance-witness pairs. Additionally, at most one of the calls to πIncrement uses ℓ = 0, and no calls to πZK uses ℓ = 0.
- During πInput, Mshared + Nshared + 2 field elements are shared by Finput.
- During πFold, 1 group element is broadcast by each party.
- During πIncrement, 1 field element and 1 group element are broadcast by each party, and 1 call to F<sup>F</sup> is made. Additionally, if ℓ = 0 during πIncrement, then |z| [3](#page-47-0) additional field elements are shared by Finput.
- During πZK, 1 field element and 1 group element are broadcast by each party. Additionally, if ℓ = 0 during πZK, then |z| additional field elements are shared by Finput.
- During πRand, 2 group elements are broadcast per party, Nshared + N mixed field elements are shared by Frand, and Nplaintext + L + 1 field elements are sampled by Fcoin.
- During πOutput, for each instance-witness pair that will be outputted, M + N + 2 field elements are broadcast by each party, and M + N + 2 field elements are shared by Finput.

Now let us sum up these statistics. First, the total number of field or group elements that are broadcast by each party is:

$$= \underbrace{2 \cdot 0}_{\pi_{\mathsf{Input}}} + \underbrace{(k+2) \cdot 1}_{\pi_{\mathsf{Fold}}} + \underbrace{k \cdot 2}_{\pi_{\mathsf{Increment}}} + \underbrace{1 \cdot 2}_{\pi_{\mathsf{ZK}}} + \underbrace{1 \cdot 2}_{\pi_{\mathsf{Rand}}} + \underbrace{2 \cdot (M+N+2)}_{\pi_{\mathsf{Output}}}$$

$$= (0) + (k+2) + (2k) + (2) + (2) + (2M+2N+4)$$

$$= 3k + 2M + 2N + 10$$

Second, the total number of field elements that are secret shared by Finput, secret shared by Frand, or sampled by Fcoin is:

$$=\underbrace{2\cdot (M^{\mathsf{shared}} + N^{\mathsf{shared}} + 2)}_{\pi_{\mathsf{Input}}} + \underbrace{(k+2)\cdot 0}_{\pi_{\mathsf{Fold}}} + \underbrace{1\cdot |z|}_{\pi_{\mathsf{Increment}}} + \underbrace{0\cdot |z|}_{\pi_{\mathsf{ZK}}} \\ + \underbrace{1\cdot (N^{\mathsf{plaintext}} + N^{\mathsf{shared}} + N^{\mathsf{mixed}} + L + 1)}_{\pi_{\mathsf{Rand}}} + \underbrace{2\cdot (M + N + 2)}_{\pi_{\mathsf{Output}}} \\ = (2M^{\mathsf{shared}} + 2N^{\mathsf{shared}} + 4) + (0) + (|z|) + (0) \\ + (N^{\mathsf{plaintext}} + N^{\mathsf{shared}} + N^{\mathsf{mixed}} + L + 1) + (2M + 2N + 4) \\ \leq (2M + 2N + 4) + (0) + (N) + (0) \\ + [N + (n+1) + 1] + (2M + 2N + 4) \\ = 4M + 6N + n + 10$$

We used the facts that Nplaintext + Nshared + N mixed ≤ N, |z| ≤ N, Mshared ≤ M, and L = n + 1. Third, the total number of calls to F<sup>F</sup> is:

$$= \underbrace{2 \cdot 0}_{\pi_{\mathsf{Input}}} + \underbrace{(k+2) \cdot 0}_{\pi_{\mathsf{Fold}}} + \underbrace{k \cdot 1}_{\pi_{\mathsf{Increment}}} + \underbrace{1 \cdot 0}_{\pi_{\mathsf{ZK}}} + \underbrace{1 \cdot 0}_{\pi_{\mathsf{Rand}}} + \underbrace{2 \cdot 0}_{\pi_{\mathsf{Output}}}$$

$$= k$$

<span id="page-47-0"></span><sup>3</sup>Let |z| be the number of field elements contained in z.

{48}------------------------------------------------

# <span id="page-48-0"></span>5 Construction of Collaborative Folding

Here we provide an MPC protocol  $\pi_{CFS}$  (Section 5.1) that securely computes the collaborative folding functionality  $\mathcal{F}_{CFS}$  (Fig. 4.4).

**Overview.** The protocol  $\pi_{CFS}$  (Section 5.1) closely resembles the functionality  $\mathcal{F}_{CFS}$  (Fig. 4.4). The main differences are the following. The provers are responsible for storing the state variables (inst<sub>A</sub>, w<sub>A</sub>, inst<sub>B</sub>, w<sub>B</sub>, inst<sub>C</sub>, w<sub>C</sub>, pk<sub>NIFS</sub>), and (w<sub>A</sub>, w<sub>B</sub>, w<sub>C</sub>) are secret-shared among the provers to hide their plaintext values from a dishonest subset of provers.

Accordingly, whenever we process  $(w_A, w_B, w_C)$ , we need MPC protocols to act on the secret-shared values. The ideal-world functions (Input, Fold, Increment, ZK, Rand) are replaced with protocols  $(\pi_{\mathsf{Input}}, \pi_{\mathsf{Fold}}, \pi_{\mathsf{Increment}}, \pi_{\mathsf{ZK}}, \pi_{\mathsf{Rand}})$  that output a secret-shared witness. Additionally, the operation Output is replaced with a protocol  $\pi_{\mathsf{Output}}$  during which the provers reconstruct a subset of  $(w_A, w_B, w_C)$  from their shares.

## **Preliminaries**

**Slicing.** Part of the witness will be secret shared using a technique we call *slicing*, shown in Fig. 5.1. One party j will hold a value  $\mathbf{v}$  locally, and all other parties will hold  $\mathbf{0}$ . This can be viewed as a form of Shamir sharing with a polynomial of degree n-1.

Figure 5.1: slice(
$$\mathbf{v}, \ell, j$$
)

- <span id="page-48-1"></span>1: **Inputs:** All parties receive an index  $j \in [n]$  and a vector length  $\ell$ . Additionally, party j receives a vector  $\mathbf{v}$  of length  $\ell$ .
- 2: Each party  $j' \in [n]$  computes

$$\{\mathbf{v}\}_{j'} = \begin{cases} \frac{1}{L_j(0)} \cdot \mathbf{v}, & j' = j\\ \mathbf{0}^{\ell}, & j' \neq j \end{cases}$$

$$(5.1)$$

Also let  $\{\mathbf{v}\} = (\{\mathbf{v}\}_{j'})_{j' \in [n]}$ .

3: Outputs: Each party  $j' \in [n]$  holds their share  $\{\mathbf{v}\}_{j'}$ .

Note that  $\{\mathbf{v}\}$  is a valid Shamir sharing of  $\mathbf{v}$  with a polynomial of degree n-1, and  $\mathbf{v}$  can be reconstructed from  $\{\mathbf{v}\}$  by the normal SSS.open procedure (Eq. (3.1)):

$$\mathbf{v} = \mathsf{SSS.open}(\mathbb{F}, \{\mathbf{v}\}) = \sum_{j \in [n]} L_j(0) \cdot \{\mathbf{v}\}_j$$

The reason that party j multiplies  $\mathbf{v}$  by  $\frac{1}{L_j(0)}$  during slice is because SSS.open( $\mathbb{F}, \{\mathbf{v}\}$ ) will multiply  $\{\mathbf{v}\}_j$  by  $L_j(0)$ .

Sharing the witness. We will break up the witness  $\mathbf{w} = (\mathbf{E}, r_{\mathbf{E}}, \mathbf{W}, r_{\mathbf{W}})$  by the subcircuits of  $H_F$  and use different sharing methods for each subcircuit. Every sharing method is some form of Shamir sharing, so every component can be reconstructed using SSS.open.

Recall that in Nova's folding scheme,  $(\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{B}, \mathbf{C})$  is the R1CS structure,  $\mathbf{T}$  is the folding cross term, and  $\mathbf{E}$  is the accumulated error term. Also recall that Section 4.2 defined the relation circuit  $H_F$  and divided it into several subcircuits  $(H^{\mathsf{plaintext}}, H^{\mathsf{shared}}, H^{\mathsf{mixed}}, H^1, \ldots, H^n)$ .

Let us also divide up  $\mathbf{E}, \mathbf{T}, \mathbf{A}, \mathbf{B}, \mathbf{C}$  by subcircuit. Recall that each entry of  $\mathbf{E}$  and  $\mathbf{T}$  and each row of  $\mathbf{A}$ ,  $\mathbf{B}$ , and  $\mathbf{C}$  corresponds to a gate in  $H_F$ . For each subcircuit label subcircuit  $\in \{\text{plaintext}, \text{shared}, \text{mixed}, 1, \ldots, n\}$ , let  $(\mathbf{E}^{\text{subcircuit}}, \mathbf{T}^{\text{subcircuit}}, \mathbf{A}^{\text{subcircuit}}, \mathbf{B}^{\text{subcircuit}}, \mathbf{C}^{\text{subcircuit}})$  be, respectively, the entries of  $\mathbf{E}$  or  $\mathbf{T}$  or the rows of  $\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{B}, \mathbf{C}$  that correspond to the subcircuit  $H^{\text{subcircuit}}$ . Additionally, let  $M^{\text{subcircuit}}$  be the length of  $\mathbf{E}^{\text{subcircuit}}$  and  $\mathbf{T}^{\text{subcircuit}}$ .

We will share the witness  $\mathbf{w} = (\mathbf{E}, r_{\mathbf{E}}, \mathbf{W}, r_{\mathbf{W}})$  as follows.  $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}$  and  $\mathbf{E}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}$  are held by each party in plaintext form. This is a Shamir sharing of degree 0.  $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{shared}}$  and  $\mathbf{E}^{\mathsf{shared}}$  are shared among the parties using Shamir sharings of degree t and 2t respectively.  $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{mixed}}$  and  $\mathbf{E}^{\mathsf{mixed}}$  will be shared with several different types of Shamir sharing (hence the name  $\mathsf{mixed}$ ). For each  $j \in [n]$ ,  $\mathbf{W}^j$  and  $\mathbf{E}^j$  will be shared using  $\mathsf{slice}(\cdot, \cdot, j)$ . Finally,  $r_{\mathbf{W}}$  and  $r_{\mathbf{E}}$  will be shared with Shamir sharings of degree n-1. For

{49}------------------------------------------------

more detail, see  $\pi_{\text{Input}}$  (Section 5.1.1), which shares w. Let [w] refer to the secret-shared witness. We use square brackets for simplicity even though not every component of w has a sharing degree of t.

Note that every component of the witness is shared using some form of Shamir sharing, so every component can be reconstructed using the same open procedure:  $w = SSS.open(\mathbb{F}, [w])$ .

The functionality  $\mathcal{F}_F$ . Here we present a functionality  $\mathcal{F}_F$  (Fig. 5.2) that computes  $z' = F(z, \vec{\omega})$  on secret-shared values. Let  $\mathbf{W}^F$  comprise the input, output, and intermediate wire values of  $F(z, \vec{\omega})$ .  $\mathcal{F}_F$  takes ( $[z], \vec{\omega}$ ) as input, computes  $z' = F(z, \vec{\omega})$ , and outputs shares of  $\mathbf{W}^F$ .  $\mathcal{F}_F$  is used in our protocol  $\pi_{\text{Increment}}$  (Section 5.1.3).

We do not specify a protocol to securely compute  $\mathcal{F}_F$  because the protocol will depend on the choice of F. It is reasonable to assume that such a protocol exists because any collaborative IVC protocol based on F should provide an MPC protocol to compute F, and typically such a protocol involves generating secret shares of the intermediate wire values of F.

## Figure 5.2: Functionality $\mathcal{F}_F$

- <span id="page-49-1"></span>1: **Inputs:** The functionality receives a sharing threshold D, a secret-shared state [z], and a plaintext witness  $\vec{\omega}$ . The functionality also receives a list  $\mathcal{C} \subset [n]$  of corrupted parties of size  $|\mathcal{C}| \leq D$ , and receives from the adversary the corrupted parties' shares  $[\mathbf{W}^F]_{\mathcal{C}}$ . We require that the values of  $[\mathbf{W}^F]_{\mathcal{C}}$  corresponding to the input z match the shares  $[z]_{\mathcal{C}}$ .
- 2: Compute

$$z = \mathsf{SSS.open}(\mathbb{F}, [z])$$

3: Compute all the input, output, and intermediate wire values of the following circuit evaluation:

$$z' = F(z, \vec{\omega})$$

Let  $\mathbf{W}^F$  comprise these wire values.

4: Secret share  $\mathbf{W}^F$  as follows:

$$[\mathbf{W}^F] = \mathsf{SSS}.\mathsf{share}\left(\mathbb{F},\mathbf{W}^F,[\mathbf{W}^F]_\mathcal{C},\mathcal{C},D\right)$$

5: Outputs: Send to each honest party  $j \in [n] \setminus \mathcal{C}$  their share  $[\mathbf{W}^F]_j$ .

## <span id="page-49-0"></span>5.1 Protocol $\pi_{\mathsf{CFS}}$

This protocol securely computes the collaborative folding functionality  $\mathcal{F}_{CFS}$  (Fig. 4.4).

## 1. Initialization:

(a) Each party  $j \in [n]$  has an internal state comprising the following variables:

$$(\mathsf{inst}_A, \mathsf{inst}_B, \mathsf{inst}_C, [\mathsf{w}_A]_j, [\mathsf{w}_B]_j, [\mathsf{w}_C]_j, \mathsf{pk}_{\mathsf{NIFS}})$$

- (b) The parties take as input a prover key  $pk_{NIFS}$  and store it in their states.
- (c) The rest of party j's state variables are initialized as follows:

$$(\mathsf{inst}_A, [\mathsf{w}_A]_i) = (\mathsf{inst}_B, [\mathsf{w}_B]_i) = (\mathsf{inst}_C, [\mathsf{w}_C]_i) = (\mathsf{inst}_\bot, \mathsf{w}_\bot)$$

- 2. **Operation:** The user can invoke the operations  $\mathsf{op} \in \{\mathsf{Input}, \mathsf{Fold}, \mathsf{Increment}, \mathsf{ZK}, \mathsf{Rand}\}$  many times and in any order. For each invocation, the parties execute the respective protocol,  $\{\pi_{\mathsf{Input}}, \pi_{\mathsf{Fold}}, \pi_{\mathsf{Increment}}, \pi_{\mathsf{ZK}}, \pi_{\mathsf{Rand}}\}$ , which are described in Sections 5.1.1 to 5.1.5.
- 3. Output: The user may invoke op = Output once at the end. The parties execute the protocol  $\pi_{\text{Output}}$  (described in Section 5.1.6) and then stop responding to future instructions from the user.

{50}------------------------------------------------

#### <span id="page-50-0"></span>5.1.1 Protocol $\pi_{\mathsf{Input}}$

This protocol takes an instance-witness pair (inst, w) and an output position  $P \in \{A, B, C\}$ , then secret shares w, and writes the result to position P: (inst<sub>P</sub>, [w]<sub>P</sub>)  $\leftarrow$  (inst, [w]).  $\pi_{\mathsf{Input}}$  is analogous to the functionality Input (Fig. 4.5).

1. Inputs: Each party holds the instance inst and witness  $\mathbf{w} = (\mathbf{E}, r_{\mathbf{E}}, \mathbf{W}, r_{\mathbf{W}})$ , where

$$\mathbf{E} = \left(\mathbf{E}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}, \mathbf{E}^{\mathsf{shared}}, \mathbf{E}^{\mathsf{mixed}}, \mathbf{E}^1, \dots, \mathbf{E}^n\right)$$
 $\mathbf{W} = \left(\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}, \mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{shared}}, \mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{mixed}}, \mathbf{W}^1, \dots, \mathbf{W}^n\right)$ 

Each party also holds a position  $P \in \{A, B, C\}$ .

- 2. The parties invoke  $\mathcal{F}_{\text{input}}$  (Fig. 3.1) with threshold t on the vectors ( $\mathbf{E}^{\text{shared}}, \mathbf{W}^{\text{shared}}, r_{\mathbf{E}}, r_{\mathbf{W}}$ ) to obtain shares ( $[\mathbf{E}^{\text{shared}}], [\mathbf{W}^{\text{shared}}], [r_{\mathbf{E}}], [r_{\mathbf{W}}]$ ).
- 3. For every  $j \in [n]$ , the parties compute:

$$\{\mathbf{W}^j\} = \operatorname{slice}(\mathbf{W}^j, N^j, j)$$
  
 $\{\mathbf{E}^j\} = \operatorname{slice}(\mathbf{E}^j, M^j, j)$ 

4. Each party  $j \in [n]$  computes:

$$\begin{aligned} \{\mathbf{W}\}_j &= \left(\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}, [\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{shared}}]_j, \mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{mixed}}, \{\mathbf{W}^1\}_j, \dots, \{\mathbf{W}^n\}_j\right) \\ \{\mathbf{E}\}_j &= \left(\mathbf{E}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}, [\mathbf{E}^{\mathsf{shared}}]_j, \mathbf{E}^{\mathsf{mixed}}, \{\mathbf{E}^1\}_j, \dots, \{\mathbf{E}^n\}_j\right) \\ [\mathbf{w}]_j &= \left(\{\mathbf{E}\}_j, [r_{\mathbf{E}}]_j, \{\mathbf{W}\}_j, [r_{\mathbf{W}}]_j\right) \end{aligned}$$

5. Each party  $j \in [n]$  overwrites their state:

$$(\mathsf{inst}_P, [\mathsf{w}_P]_i) \leftarrow (\mathsf{inst}, [\mathsf{w}]_i)$$

#### <span id="page-50-1"></span>5.1.2 Protocol $\pi_{\mathsf{Fold}}$

This protocol folds two instance-witness pairs together. It takes as input two positions  $P, Q \in \{A, B, C\}$ , then folds together (inst<sub>P</sub>, [w<sub>P</sub>]) and (inst<sub>Q</sub>, [w<sub>Q</sub>]), and writes the result to position A.  $\pi_{\mathsf{Fold}}$  is analogous to the functionality  $\mathsf{Fold}$  (Fig. 4.6).

1. **Inputs:** Each party  $j \in [n]$  receives two positions  $P, Q \in \{A, B, C\}$ . Party j also has shares of two witnesses

$$[\mathbf{w}_{P}]_{j} = (\{\mathbf{E}_{P}\}_{j}, \{r_{\mathbf{E}_{P}}\}_{j}, \{\mathbf{W}_{P}\}_{j}, \{r_{\mathbf{W}_{P}}\}_{j})$$

$$[\mathbf{w}_{Q}]_{j} = (\{\mathbf{E}_{Q}\}_{j}, \{r_{\mathbf{E}_{Q}}\}_{j}, \{\mathbf{W}_{Q}\}_{j}, \{r_{\mathbf{W}_{Q}}\}_{j})$$

two plaintext instances

$$inst_P = (\overline{E}_P, u_P, \overline{W}_P, \mathsf{x}_P)$$
$$inst_Q = (\overline{E}_Q, u_Q, \overline{W}_Q, \mathsf{x}_Q)$$

and the prover key

$$pk_{NIFS} = (pp_{NIFS}, A, B, C, vk_{NIFS})$$

2. Each party j computes the following:

$$\begin{split} \{\mathbf{Z}_P\}_j &= \left(\mathbf{W}_P^{\mathsf{plaintext}}, [\mathbf{W}_P^{\mathsf{shared}}]_j, \{\mathbf{W}_P^{\mathsf{mixed}}\}_j, L_j(0) \cdot \left(\{\mathbf{W}_P^{j'}\}_j\right)_{j' \in [n]}, \mathsf{x}_P, u_P\right) \\ \{\mathbf{Z}_Q\}_j &= \left(\mathbf{W}_Q^{\mathsf{plaintext}}, [\mathbf{W}_Q^{\mathsf{shared}}]_j, \{\mathbf{W}_Q^{\mathsf{mixed}}\}_j, L_j(0) \cdot \left(\{\mathbf{W}_Q^{j'}\}_j\right)_{j' \in [n]}, \mathsf{x}_Q, u_Q\right) \\ \{\mathbf{T}\}_j &= \mathbf{A} \cdot \{\mathbf{Z}_P\}_j \circ \mathbf{B} \cdot \{\mathbf{Z}_Q\}_j + \mathbf{A} \cdot \{\mathbf{Z}_Q\}_j \circ \mathbf{B} \cdot \{\mathbf{Z}_P\}_j \\ &- u_P \cdot \mathbf{C} \cdot \{\mathbf{Z}_Q\}_j - u_Q \cdot \mathbf{C} \cdot \{\mathbf{Z}_P\}_j \end{split}$$

Then for each  $j' \in [n]$ , party j overwrites  $\{\mathbf{T}^{j'}\}_j$ :

$$\{\mathbf{T}^{j'}\}_j \leftarrow \frac{\mathbb{1}_{j'=j}}{L_j(0)} \cdot \{\mathbf{T}^{j'}\}_j$$

{51}------------------------------------------------

3. Each party j samples  $\{r_{\mathbf{T}}\}_j \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}$  and computes

$$\left\{\overline{T}\right\}_{j} = \mathsf{Ped.Commit}(\mathsf{pp}_{E}, \{\mathbf{T}\}_{j}; \{r_{\mathbf{T}}\}_{j})$$

- 4. The parties broadcast their shares of  $\{\overline{T}\}$  and compute  $\overline{T} = SSS.open(\mathbb{G}, \{\overline{T}\})$ .
- 5. Each party locally computes:

$$r = \mathsf{RO}(\mathsf{vk}_{\mathsf{NIFS}}, \mathsf{inst}_P, \mathsf{inst}_Q, \overline{T})$$

6. The parties locally compute the folded instance inst as follows:

$$\begin{array}{rclcrcl} \overline{E} & = & \overline{E}_P & + & r \cdot \overline{T} & + & r^2 \cdot \overline{E}_Q \\ \\ u & = & u_P & + & r \cdot u_Q \\ \\ \overline{W} & = & \overline{W}_P & + & r \cdot \overline{W}_Q \\ \\ \mathbf{x} & = & \mathbf{x}_P & + & r \cdot \mathbf{x}_Q \end{array}$$

and finally

$$inst = (\overline{E}, u, \overline{W}, x)$$

7. Each party j computes their share of the folded witness  $[w]_j$  as follows:

$$\begin{aligned} \{\mathbf{E}\}_j &\leftarrow \{\mathbf{E}_P\}_j &+ r \cdot \{\mathbf{T}\}_j &+ r^2 \cdot \{\mathbf{E}_Q\}_j \\ \{r_{\mathbf{E}}\}_j &\leftarrow \{r_{\mathbf{E}_P}\}_j &+ r \cdot \{r_{\mathbf{T}}\}_j &+ r^2 \cdot \{r_{\mathbf{E}_Q}\}_j \\ \{\mathbf{W}\}_j &\leftarrow \{\mathbf{W}_P\}_j &+ r \cdot \{\mathbf{W}_Q\}_j \\ \{r_{\mathbf{W}}\}_j &\leftarrow \{r_{\mathbf{W}_P}\}_j &+ r \cdot \{r_{\mathbf{W}_Q}\}_j \end{aligned}$$

and finally

$$[w]_j = (\{E\}_j, \{r_E\}_j, \{W\}_j, \{r_W\}_j)$$

8. Outputs: Each party j overwrites position A with the folded pair as follows:

$$(\mathsf{inst}_A, [\mathsf{w}_A]_i) \leftarrow (\mathsf{inst}, [\mathsf{w}]_i)$$

The parties also output  $\operatorname{inst}_A$  and  $\pi' = \overline{T}$ .

#### <span id="page-51-0"></span>5.1.3 Protocol $\pi_{\mathsf{Increment}}$

This protocol extends the IVC for one additional increment. This entails computing  $z' = F(z, \vec{\omega})$  and computing  $H_F$  in increment mode (mode' = increment) to generate a proof for the latest increment.  $\pi_{\text{Increment}}$  is constructed in the  $\mathcal{F}_F$ -hybrid model (Fig. 5.2) and is analogous to the functionality Increment (Fig. 4.7).

- 1. **Inputs:** Each party  $j \in [n]$  has external inputs  $(\ell, z_0, \omega^j, \mathsf{Inst}, \mathsf{inst}, \pi)$  as well as a state that includes  $(\mathsf{pk}_{\mathsf{NIFS}}, \mathsf{inst}_B, [\mathsf{w}_B]_j)$ .
- 2. From  $pk_{NIFS}$  each party reads the keys  $(pp_E, pp_W, vk_{NIFS})$  and computes  $inst_{\perp}$  according to Eq. (3.3).
- 3. If  $\ell = 0$ :
  - (a) The parties invoke  $\mathcal{F}_{input}$  with threshold t and value  $z_0$  to obtain  $\mathbf{z} = \mathsf{SSS.Share}(\mathbb{F}, z_0, [z_0]_{\mathcal{C}}, \mathcal{C}, t)$ . Each party j receives  $z_j$ .
  - (b) Each party j sets (mode,  $[z]_j$ ,  $\mathbf{h}$ ,  $\mathbf{r}$ ) = (increment,  $z_j$ ,  $\mathbf{0}^{n+1}$ ,  $\mathbf{0}^n$ ). This ensures that  $[z] = \mathbf{z}$ .
- 4. Otherwise (if  $\ell \geq 1$ ), each party j does the following:
  - (a) Read from  $(inst_B, [w_B]_j)$  the values  $(inst_B, [w_B]_j).(mode', z'_j, \mathbf{h}', r'_j)$ . This entails multiplying the shares of  $(inst_B, [w_B]_j).(z'_j, r'_j)$  by  $L_j(0)$  because these values are shared using slicing.

{52}------------------------------------------------

(b) Rename the variables as follows:

$$(\mathsf{mode},[z]_j,z_j,\mathbf{h},r_j) \leftarrow (\mathsf{inst}_B,[\mathsf{w}_B]_j).(\mathsf{mode}',z_i',z_i',\mathbf{h}',r_i')$$

This ensures that  $[z] = \mathbf{z}$ .

#### 5. W<sup>shared</sup>:

- (a) The parties invoke  $\mathcal{F}_F$  with input D = t (Fig. 5.2). Each party j also provides the inputs  $([z]_j, \omega^j)$ . If j is corrupted, then they also provide input  $[\mathbf{W}^F]_j$ .
- (b)  $\mathcal{F}_F$  sends to each honest party j their share  $[\mathbf{W}^F]_j$ .
- (c) Each party j computes  $[\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{shared}}]_j$  (defined in step 2) using  $[\mathbf{W}^F]_j$ . This entails setting  $[z_{\mathsf{in}}, \vec{\omega}_{\mathsf{in}}, z_{\mathsf{out}}]_j = [z, \vec{\omega}, z']_j$ .
- (d) Each party j reads  $[z']_j$  from  $[\mathbf{W}^F]_j$  and sets  $z'_j = [z']_j$  (so that  $\mathbf{z}' = [z']$ ).
- 6. Each party  $j \in [n]$  samples  $\{r_{\mathbf{W}}\}_j, r'_j \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}$  independently.
- 7. Each party sets

$$\mathsf{mode}' = \mathsf{increment}$$
  
 $(\mathsf{inst}', \pi') = (\mathsf{inst}_{\perp}, 0)$ 

8. W<sup>plaintext</sup>: Each party computes W<sup>plaintext</sup> to be the wire values of

$$H^{\mathsf{plaintext}}$$
 (vk<sub>NIFS</sub>, mode, mode',  $\ell, z_0$ , inst <sub>$\perp$</sub> , Inst, inst',  $\pi, \pi', \mathbf{h}$ )

 $(H^{\mathsf{plaintext}} \text{ is defined in step 1}).$  This involves computing  $h'_0$ .

- 9. W<sup>mixed</sup>:
  - (a) For each  $j \in [n]$ , the parties (locally) compute the sharings:

$$\{z_j\} = \operatorname{slice}(z_j, 1, j)$$
  
 $\{z'_j\} = \operatorname{slice}(z'_j, 1, j)$ 

This can be computed locally because each party j knows  $z_j$  and  $z'_j$ .

- (b) Additionally, let  $\{\mathbf{z}\} = (\{z_1\}, \dots, \{z_n\})$  and  $\{\mathbf{z}'\} = (\{z_1'\}, \dots, \{z_n'\})$ .
- (c) The parties compute  $H^{\mathsf{mixed}}$  (defined in step 3) homomorphically by locally applying  $H^{\mathsf{mixed}}$  to their shares  $([z], [z'], \{\mathbf{z}\}, \{\mathbf{z}'\})$  instead of to  $(z, z', \mathbf{z}, \mathbf{z}')$ . For each party j, let  $\{\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{mixed}}\}_j$  be the wire values of the circuit  $H^{\mathsf{mixed}}$  applied to  $([z]_j, [z']_j, \{\mathbf{z}\}_j, \{\mathbf{z}'\}_j)$ .

## 10. $W^{j}$ :

(a) Each party  $j \in [n]$  computes  $\mathbf{W}^j$  to be the inputs and intermediate wire values of

$$H^j(h_j,z_j,r_j,z_j',r_j')$$

 $(H^j)$  is defined in step 4). This involves computing  $h'_i$ .

- (b) For each  $j \in [n]$ , the parties (locally) compute  $\{\mathbf{W}^j\} = \mathsf{slice}(\mathbf{W}^j, N^j, j)$  (Fig. 5.1).
- 11. Each party j computes:

$$\begin{split} \{\mathbf{W}\}_j &= \left(\mathbf{W}^{\text{plaintext}}, [\mathbf{W}^{\text{shared}}]_j, \{\mathbf{W}^{\text{mixed}}\}_j, \{\mathbf{W}^1\}_j, \dots, \{\mathbf{W}^n\}_j \right) \\ (\mathbf{E}, r_{\mathbf{E}}, u) &= \left(\mathbf{0}^M, 0, 1\right) \\ [\mathbf{w}]_j &= (\mathbf{E}, r_{\mathbf{E}}, \{\mathbf{W}\}_j, \{r_{\mathbf{W}}\}_j) \\ \overline{E} &= \mathsf{Ped.Commit}(\mathsf{pp}_E, \mathbf{E}; r_{\mathbf{E}}) \\ \{\overline{W}\}_j &= \mathsf{Ped.Commit}(\mathsf{pp}_W, \{\mathbf{W}\}_j; \{r_{\mathbf{W}}\}_j) \end{split}$$

12. Each party j broadcasts  $h'_j$  to the other parties. Then they broadcast  $\{\overline{W}\}_j$  to the other parties.

{53}------------------------------------------------

13. Each party computes:

$$\mathbf{x} = \mathbf{h}' = (h'_0, h'_1, \dots, h'_n)$$
 
$$\overline{W} = \mathsf{SSS.open}(\mathbb{G}, \{\overline{W}\})$$
 
$$\mathsf{inst} = (\overline{E}, u, \overline{W}, \mathsf{x})$$

14. **Output:** Each party j overwrites position B with the new pair as follows:

$$(\mathsf{inst}_B, [\mathsf{w}_B]_i) \leftarrow (\mathsf{inst}, [\mathsf{w}]_i)$$

The parties also output inst.

## <span id="page-53-0"></span>5.1.4 Protocol $\pi_{ZK}$

This protocol performs several steps needed to ensure the IVC proof satisfies t-zero-knowledge, such as folding together three instances (Inst, inst, inst').  $\pi_{ZK}$  is analogous to the functionality ZK (Fig. 4.8).

- 1. **Inputs:** Each party  $j \in [n]$  has external inputs  $(\ell, z_0, \mathsf{Inst}, \mathsf{inst}', \pi, \pi')$  as well as a state that includes  $(\mathsf{pk}_{\mathsf{NIFS}}, \mathsf{inst}_B, [\mathsf{w}_B]_j)$ .
- 2. From  $pk_{NIFS}$  each party reads the keys  $(pp_E, pp_W, vk_{NIFS})$  and computes  $inst_{\perp}$  according to Eq. (3.3).
- 3. If  $\ell = 0$ :
  - (a) The parties invoke  $\mathcal{F}_{input}$  (Fig. 3.1) with threshold t and value  $z_0$  to obtain  $\mathbf{z} = \mathsf{SSS.Share}(\mathbb{F}, z_0, [z_0]_{\mathcal{C}}, \mathcal{C}, t)$ . Each party j receives  $z_j$ .
  - (b) Each party j sets (mode,  $[z]_j$ ,  $\mathbf{h}$ ,  $\mathbf{r}$ ) = (increment,  $z_j$ ,  $\mathbf{0}^{n+1}$ ,  $\mathbf{0}^n$ ). This ensures that  $[z] = \mathbf{z}$ .
- 4. Otherwise (if  $\ell \geq 1$ ), each party j does the following:
  - (a) Read from  $(inst_B, [w_B]_j)$  the values  $(inst_B, [w_B]_j).(mode', z'_j, \mathbf{h}', r'_j)$ . This entails multiplying the shares of  $(inst_B, [w_B]_j).(z'_j, r'_j)$  by  $L_j(0)$  because these values are shared using slicing.
  - (b) Rename the variables as follows:

$$(\mathsf{mode}, [z]_j, z_j, \mathbf{h}, r_j) \leftarrow (\mathsf{inst}_B, [\mathsf{w}_B]_j).(\mathsf{mode}', z_i', z_i', \mathbf{h}', r_i')$$

This ensures that  $[z] = \mathbf{z}$ .

5. Each party j sets

$$[z']_j = z'_j = [z]_j$$

so that 
$$[z'] = \mathbf{z}' = [z] = \mathbf{z}$$
.

- 6. Each party  $j \in [n]$  samples  $\{r_{\mathbf{W}}\}_j, r'_i \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}$  independently.
- 7. Each party sets

$$mode' = zk$$

$$\vec{\omega} = \mathbf{0}$$

8. W<sup>plaintext</sup>: Each party computes W<sup>plaintext</sup> to be the wire values of

$$H^{\mathsf{plaintext}}\left(\mathsf{vk}_{\mathsf{NIFS}},\mathsf{mode},\mathsf{mode}',\ell,z_0,\mathsf{inst}_\perp,\mathsf{Inst},\mathsf{inst}',\pi,\pi',\mathbf{h}\right)$$

 $(H^{\text{plaintext}} \text{ is defined in step 1}).$  This involves computing  $h'_0$ .

9. W<sup>shared</sup>: Each party j computes  $[\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{shared}}]_j$  to be the wire values of

$$H^{\mathsf{shared}}\left(\mathsf{mode}',[z]_j,\vec{\omega}\right)$$

 $(H^{\mathsf{shared}} \text{ is defined in step 2}).$  This entails setting  $(z_{\mathsf{in}}, \vec{\omega}_{\mathsf{in}}, z_{\mathsf{out}}) = (0, \mathbf{0}, F(0, \mathbf{0}))$  and  $[z']_j = [z]_j$ .

{54}------------------------------------------------

- 10. Wmixed:
  - (a) For each j ∈ [n], the parties (locally) compute the sharings:

$$\begin{aligned} \{z_j\} &= \mathsf{slice}(z_j, 1, j) \\ \{z_j'\} &= \mathsf{slice}(z_j', 1, j) \end{aligned}$$

This can be computed locally because each party j knows z<sup>j</sup> and z ′ j .

- (b) Additionally, let {z} = ({z1}, . . . , {zn}) and {z ′} = ({z ′ <sup>1</sup>}, . . . , {z ′ <sup>n</sup>}).
- (c) The parties compute Hmixed (defined in [step 3\)](#page-25-3) homomorphically by locally applying Hmixed to their shares ([z], [z ′ ], {z}, {z ′}) instead of to (z, z′ , z, z ′ ). For each party j, let {Wmixed}<sup>j</sup> be the wire values of the circuit Hmixed applied to ([z]<sup>j</sup> , [z ′ ]<sup>j</sup> , {z}<sup>j</sup> , {z ′}<sup>j</sup> ).

#### 11. W<sup>j</sup> :

(a) Each party j ∈ [n] computes W<sup>j</sup> to be the inputs and intermediate wire values of

$$H^j(h_j, z_j, r_j, z'_j, r'_j)$$

(H<sup>j</sup> is defined in [step 4\)](#page-25-1). This involves computing h ′ j .

- (b) For each j ∈ [n], the parties (locally) compute {W<sup>j</sup>} = slice(W<sup>j</sup> , N<sup>j</sup> , j) [\(Fig. 5.1\)](#page-48-1).
- 12. Each party j computes:

$$\begin{split} \{\mathbf{W}\}_j &= \left(\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}, [\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{shared}}]_j, \{\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{mixed}}\}_j, \{\mathbf{W}^1\}_j, \dots, \{\mathbf{W}^n\}_j \right) \\ (\mathbf{E}, r_{\mathbf{E}}, u) &= \left(\mathbf{0}^M, 0, 1\right) \\ [\mathbf{w}]_j &= (\mathbf{E}, r_{\mathbf{E}}, \{\mathbf{W}\}_j, \{r_{\mathbf{W}}\}_j) \\ \overline{E} &= \mathsf{Ped.Commit}(\mathsf{pp}_E, \mathbf{E}; r_{\mathbf{E}}) \\ \{\overline{W}\}_j &= \mathsf{Ped.Commit}(\mathsf{pp}_W, \{\mathbf{W}\}_j; \{r_{\mathbf{W}}\}_j) \end{split}$$

- 13. Each party j broadcasts h ′ j to the other parties. Then they broadcast {W}<sup>j</sup> to the other parties.
- 14. Each party computes:

$$\mathbf{x} = \mathbf{h}' = (h_0', h_1', \dots, h_n')$$
 
$$\overline{W} = \mathsf{SSS.open}(\mathbb{G}, \{\overline{W}\})$$
 
$$\mathsf{inst} = (\overline{E}, u, \overline{W}, \mathbf{x})$$

15. Output: Each party j overwrites position B with the new instance-witness pair as follows:

$$(\mathsf{inst}_B, [\mathsf{w}_B]_j) \leftarrow (\mathsf{inst}, [\mathsf{w}]_j)$$

The parties also output inst.

## <span id="page-54-0"></span>5.1.5 Protocol πRand

This protocol generates shares of a random accepting witness. πRand is analogous to Rand [\(Fig. 4.9\)](#page-30-1).

- 1. Inputs: Each party has pkNIFS.
- 2. From pkNIFS, the parties read the commitment keys (ppE, pp<sup>W</sup> ), the R1CS structure (A, B, C), and the parameters (L, M, N).
- 3. The parties call Fcoin [\(Fig. 3.3\)](#page-10-2) on input Nplaintext, and they all receive Wplaintext \$ ← F Nplaintext .
- 4. The parties call Frand [\(Fig. 3.2\)](#page-10-3) with vector length Nshared + N mixed. They receive sharings [Wshared], [Wmixed] for uniformly random vectors (Wshared ,Wmixed) \$ ← F Nshared × F Nmixed .
- 5. Each party j samples {rE}<sup>j</sup> , {rW}<sup>j</sup> ,W<sup>j</sup> \$ ← F × F × F N<sup>j</sup> independently.

{55}------------------------------------------------

- 6. The parties call  $\mathcal{F}_{\text{coin}}$  on input L+1, and they all receive  $(\mathsf{x},u) \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}^L \times \mathbb{F}$ .
- 7. The parties locally compute:

$$\begin{split} \{\mathbf{W}^j\} &= \mathsf{slice}(\mathbf{W}^j, N^j, j), \quad \forall j \in [n] \\ \{\mathbf{W}\} &= \left(\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}, \left[\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{shared}}\right], \left[\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{mixed}}\right], \{\mathbf{W}^1\}, \dots, \{\mathbf{W}^n\}\right) \end{split}$$

8. Each party  $j \in [n]$  computes the following:

$$\begin{aligned} \{\mathbf{Z}\}_j &= \left(\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}, [\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{shared}}]_j, [\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{mixed}}]_j, L_j(0) \cdot \left(\{\mathbf{W}^{j'}\}_j\right)_{j' \in [n]}, \mathsf{x}, u\right) \\ \{\mathbf{E}\}_j &= \mathbf{A} \cdot \{\mathbf{Z}\}_j \circ \mathbf{B} \cdot \{\mathbf{Z}\}_j - u \cdot \mathbf{C} \cdot \{\mathbf{Z}\}_j \end{aligned}$$

Then for each  $j' \in [n]$ , party j overwrites  $\{\mathbf{E}^{j'}\}_j$ :

$$\{\mathbf{E}^{j'}\}_j \leftarrow \frac{\mathbb{1}_{j'=j}}{L_j(0)} \cdot \{\mathbf{E}^{j'}\}_j$$

9. Each party  $j \in [n]$  computes

$$\begin{split} &\{\overline{E}\}_j = \mathsf{Ped.Commit}(\mathsf{pp}_E, \{\mathbf{E}\}_j; \{r_{\mathbf{E}}\}_j) \\ &\{\overline{W}\}_j = \mathsf{Ped.Commit}(\mathsf{pp}_W, \{\mathbf{W}\}_j; \{r_{\mathbf{W}}\}_j) \\ &[\mathsf{w}]_j = (\{\mathbf{E}\}_j, \{r_{\mathbf{E}}\}_j, \{\mathbf{W}\}_j, \{r_{\mathbf{W}}\}_j) \end{split}$$

10. The parties broadcast their shares of  $\{\overline{E}\} = (\{\overline{E}\}_1, \dots, \{\overline{E}\}_n)$  and  $\{\overline{W}\} = (\{\overline{W}\}_1, \dots, \{\overline{W}\}_n)$  and reconstruct by computing:

$$\overline{E} = \mathsf{SSS.open}\left(\mathbb{G}, \{\overline{E}\}\right)$$
 
$$\overline{W} = \mathsf{SSS.open}\left(\mathbb{G}, \{\overline{W}\}\right)$$

11. The parties compute

$$\mathsf{inst} = (\overline{E}, u, \overline{W}, \mathsf{x})$$

12. Outputs: Each party  $j \in [n]$  overwrites position C with the new instance-witness pair as follows:

$$(\mathsf{inst}_C, [\mathsf{w}_C]_j) \leftarrow (\mathsf{inst}, [\mathsf{w}]_j)$$

The parties also output inst.

#### <span id="page-55-0"></span>5.1.6 Protocol $\pi_{\text{Output}}$

This protocol reconstructs and outputs any subset of the instance-witness pairs stored by the parties.  $\pi_{\text{Output}}$  is analogous to the functionality Output (Fig. 4.10).

- 1. **Inputs:** The parties receive a list  $L \subseteq \{A, B, C\}$  of positions to output.
- 2. For each position  $P \in L$ , the parties do the following:
  - (a) The parties invoke  $\mathcal{F}_{input}$  (Fig. 3.1) with threshold D = n 1 and value  $\mathbf{0}^{M+1+N+1}$  to obtain a sharing  $\{\mathbf{0}\}$ .
  - (b) The parties compute

$$[w_P]' = [w_P] + \{0\}$$

(c) The parties broadcast their shares of  $[w_P]'$  and reconstruct using SSS.open. They obtain

$$w_P = SSS.open(\mathbb{F}, [w_P]')$$

(d) **Outputs:** The parties output (inst $_P$ ,  $w_P$ ).

{56}------------------------------------------------

## <span id="page-56-0"></span>5.2 Security Proof

<span id="page-56-2"></span>We now prove the following theorem, which states that  $\pi_{CFS}$  securely computes  $\mathcal{F}_{CFS}$ .

Theorem 31. The collaborative folding protocol  $\pi_{\mathsf{CFS}}$  (Section 5.1) securely computes the ideal functionality  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{CFS}}$  (Fig. 4.4) with abort in the  $(\mathcal{F}_{input}, \mathcal{F}_{rand}, \mathcal{F}_{coin}, \mathcal{F}_{F})$ -hybrid model (Figs. 3.1 to 3.3 and 5.2) in the presence of a malicious or semi-honest adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  controlling  $t < \frac{n}{2}$  parties.

The rest of Section 5.2 is devoted to proving Theorem 31.

## <span id="page-56-1"></span>5.2.1 The Simulator $\mathcal S$

Given any real-world adversary  $\mathcal{A}$  that corrupts a subset  $\mathcal{C}$  of parties, we will construct a simulator  $\mathcal{S}$  that simulates the view of  $\mathcal{A}$  and operates in the ideal world with access to  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{CFS}}$  (Fig. 4.4).  $\mathcal{S}$  is parametrized by  $\mathcal{A}$  and  $\mathcal{C}$ .

Let  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$  be the semi-honest adversary that simply follows the protocol, and let  $\mathcal{A}$  be the actual adversary, who may or may not be the same as  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$ .

### 1. Initialization:

- (a)  ${\mathcal S}$  takes as input a prover key  ${\sf pk}_{\sf NIFS}$  and initializes  ${\mathcal F}_{\sf CFS}$  on inputs  ${\sf pk}_{\sf NIFS}$  and  ${\mathcal C}$ .
- (b) S samples a random tape r for the adversary.
- (c) S initializes  $A^{SH}$  and A on inputs  $pk_{NIFS}$  and r, and runs  $A^{SH}$  and A internally.
- <span id="page-56-3"></span>2. **Open:** Whenever the protocol opens a degree-(n-1) sharing  $\{x\}$ ,  $\mathcal{S}$  simulates the opening as follows.
  - (a) S has  $x \in \mathbb{G}$  or  $\mathbb{F}$  and receives  $\{x\}_{\mathcal{C}}^{\mathsf{SH}}$  from  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$ .
  - (b) S samples shares for the honest parties  $\{x\}_{\mathcal{H}}$  uniformly at random such that

$$x = \mathsf{SSS.open}\left(\{\mathbb{F},\mathbb{G}\},(\{x\}_{\mathcal{H}},\{x\}_{\mathcal{C}}^{\mathsf{SH}})\right)$$

- (c) S sends  $\{x\}_{\mathcal{H}}$  to A and  $A^{SH}$ .
- (d)  $\mathcal{A}$  outputs their shares  $\{x\}_{\mathcal{C}}$ .
- (e) S computes

$$x' = \mathsf{SSS.open}\left(\{\mathbb{F}, \mathbb{G}\}, (\{x\}_{\mathcal{H}}, \{x\}_{\mathcal{C}})\right)$$

- (f) S overwrites the value of x that  $A^{SH}$  recontructed with the value x'.
- 3. **Operation:** S can be invoked on the operations  $op \in \{Input, Fold, Increment, ZK, Rand\}$  many times and in any order. Each time S is invoked on an operation op, S sends op to the ideal functionality, A, and  $A^{SH}$ , and then handles the operation as follows.
  - (a) If op = Input:
    - i. S receives the corrupted parties' inputs, (inst, w, P), and sends them to the ideal functionality, A, and  $A^{SH}$ .
    - ii. (The parties in  $\pi_{\mathsf{Input}}$  call  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{input}}$ ).  $\mathcal{A}$  outputs their shares

$$\left( [\mathbf{E}^{\mathsf{shared}}]_{\mathcal{C}}, [\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{shared}}]_{\mathcal{C}}, [r_{\mathbf{E}}]_{\mathcal{C}}, [r_{\mathbf{W}}]_{\mathcal{C}} \right).$$

- (b) If op = Fold:
  - i. S receives the corrupted parties' inputs, (P,Q), and sends them to the ideal functionality, A, and  $A^{\mathsf{SH}}$ .
  - ii. S receives  $\pi = \overline{T}$  from the functionality.
  - iii. S simulates the opening of  $\{\overline{T}\}$  using the **Open** operation and obtains  $\overline{T}'$ .
  - iv. S sends  $\pi' = \overline{T}'$  to the functionality.
- (c) If op = Increment:

{57}------------------------------------------------

i. S receives the corrupted parties' external inputs

$$(\ell, z_0, \vec{\omega}^{\mathcal{C}}, \mathsf{Inst}, \mathsf{inst}, \pi),$$

and sends these inputs to  $\mathcal{A}$  and  $\mathcal{A}^{SH}$ .

- ii. (The parties in  $\pi_{\text{Increment}}$  invoke  $\mathcal{F}_{\text{input}}$ ). If  $\ell = 0$ ,  $\mathcal{A}$  outputs their shares  $[z_0]_{\mathcal{C}}$ .
- iii. (The parties in  $\pi_{\mathsf{Increment}}$  invoke  $\mathcal{F}_F$ ).  $\mathcal{A}$  outputs  $[\mathbf{W}^F]_{\mathcal{C}}$  and  $\vec{\omega}'^{\mathcal{C}}$  (the values they output in place of  $\vec{\omega}^{\mathcal{C}}$ ).  $\mathcal{S}$  reads from  $[\mathbf{W}^F]_{\mathcal{C}}$  the shares  $[z']_{\mathcal{C}}$ .  $\mathcal{S}$  sets  $\mathbf{z}'_{\mathcal{C}} = [z']_{\mathcal{C}}$ .
- iv. (Each party j in  $\pi_{\mathsf{Increment}}$  samples  $r'_j$ ).  $\mathcal{S}$  reads from the adversary's random tape the values  $\mathbf{r}'_{\mathcal{C}}$  that  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$  samples.
- v. S sends to the ideal functionality the inputs  $(\ell, z_0, \vec{\omega}'^{\mathcal{C}}, \mathsf{Inst}, \mathsf{inst}, \pi, \mathbf{r}'_{\mathcal{C}}, \mathbf{z}'_{\mathcal{C}})$ . If  $\ell = 0$ , then S also sends  $[z_0]_{\mathcal{C}}$  to the functionality. Then the ideal functionality sends back  $\mathsf{inst} = (\overline{E}, u, \overline{W}, \mathsf{x})$ . Note that  $\mathsf{x} = \mathsf{h}' = (h'_0, h'_1, \ldots, h'_n)$ .
- vi. (Each party j in  $\pi_{\mathsf{Increment}}$  broadcasts  $h'_j$ ).  $\mathcal{S}$  sends  $\mathbf{h}'_{\mathcal{H}}$  to  $\mathcal{A}$ , and  $\mathcal{A}$  responds with  $\mathbf{h}''_{\mathcal{C}}$ .  $\mathcal{S}$  sets  $\mathbf{x}' = (h'_0, \mathbf{h}'_{\mathcal{H}}, \mathbf{h}''_{\mathcal{C}})$ . Then  $\mathcal{S}$  overwrites the value of  $\mathbf{x}$  that  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$  stores with the value  $\mathbf{x}'$ .
- vii. (Each party j in  $\pi_{\mathsf{Increment}}$  broadcasts  $\{\overline{W}\}_j$ ).  $\mathcal{S}$  simulates the opening of  $\{\overline{W}\}$  using the **Open** operation and obtains  $\overline{W}'$ .
- viii. S computes  $inst' = (\overline{E}, u, \overline{W}', x')$  and sends inst' to the functionality.
- (d) If op = ZK:
  - i. S receives the corrupted parties' external inputs

$$(\ell, z_0, \mathsf{Inst}, \mathsf{inst}, \mathsf{inst}', \pi, \pi'),$$

and sends these inputs to  $\mathcal{A}$  and  $\mathcal{A}^{SH}$ .

- ii. (The parties in  $\pi_{\mathsf{ZK}}$  invoke  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{input}}$ ). If  $\ell = 0$ ,  $\mathcal{A}$  outputs their shares  $[z_0]_{\mathcal{C}}$ .
- iii. (Each party j in  $\pi_{\mathsf{ZK}}$  samples  $r'_j$ ).  $\mathcal{S}$  reads from the adversary's random tape the values  $\mathbf{r}'_{\mathcal{C}}$  that  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$  samples.
- iv. S sends to the ideal functionality the inputs  $(\ell, z_0, \mathsf{Inst}, \mathsf{inst}, \mathsf{inst}', \pi, \pi', \mathbf{r}'_{\mathcal{C}})$ . If  $\ell = 0$ , then S also sends  $[z_0]_{\mathcal{C}}$  to the functionality. Then the ideal functionality sends back  $\mathsf{inst} = (\overline{E}, u, \overline{W}, \mathsf{x})$ . Note that  $\mathsf{x} = \mathsf{h}' = (h'_0, h'_1, \ldots, h'_n)$ .
- v. (Each party j in  $\pi_{ZK}$  broadcasts  $h'_j$ ). S sends  $\mathbf{h}'_{\mathcal{H}}$  to A, and A responds with  $\mathbf{h}''_{\mathcal{C}}$ . S sets  $\mathbf{x}' = (h'_0, \mathbf{h}'_{\mathcal{H}}, \mathbf{h}''_{\mathcal{C}})$ . Then S overwrites the value of  $\mathbf{x}$  that  $A^{SH}$  stores with the value  $\mathbf{x}'$ .
- vi. (Each party j in  $\pi_{\mathsf{ZK}}$  broadcasts  $\{\overline{W}\}_j$ ).  $\mathcal{S}$  simulates the opening of  $\{\overline{W}\}$  using the **Open** operation and obtains  $\overline{W}'$ .
- vii. S computes  $inst' = (\overline{E}, u, \overline{W}', x')$  and sends inst' to the functionality.
- (e) If op = Rand:
  - i. (The parties in  $\pi_{\mathsf{Rand}}$  call  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{coin}}$  to sample  $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}$ ).  $\mathcal{S}$  samples  $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}} \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}^{N^{\mathsf{plaintext}}}$  and sends  $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}$  to  $\mathcal{A}$  and  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$ .
  - ii. (The parties in  $\pi_{\mathsf{Rand}}$  call  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{rand}}$  to sample  $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{shared}}$  and  $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{mixed}}$ ).  $\mathcal{A}$  outputs the shares  $[\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{shared}}]_{\mathcal{C}}, [\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{mixed}}]_{\mathcal{C}}$ .
  - iii. (Each party j in  $\pi_{\mathsf{Rand}}$  samples  $\mathbf{W}^j$ ).  $\mathcal{S}$  reads from the random tape r the values of  $(\mathbf{W}^j)_{j\in\mathcal{C}}$  that  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$  would sample.
  - iv. S sends  $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}$  and  $(\mathbf{W}^j)_{j \in \mathcal{C}}$  to the functionality. Then the functionality sends back  $\mathsf{inst} = (\overline{E}, u, \overline{W}, \mathsf{x}).$
  - v. (The parties in  $\pi_{\mathsf{Rand}}$  call  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{coin}}$  to sample  $(\mathsf{x}, u)$ ).  $\mathcal{S}$  reads  $(\mathsf{x}, u)$  from inst and sends  $(\mathsf{x}, u)$  to  $\mathcal{A}$  and  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$ .
  - vi. S simulates the opening of  $\{\overline{E}\}$  and  $\{\overline{W}\}$  using the **Open** operation and obtains  $\overline{E}', \overline{W}'$ .
  - vii.  $\mathcal{S}$  computes  $\mathsf{inst}' = (\overline{E}', u, \overline{W}', \mathsf{x})$  and sends  $\mathsf{inst}'$  to the functionality.
- 4. **Output:** The first time that S is invoked on op = Output, S handles it as follows. Afterward, S stops responding to future op instructions.
  - (a) S receives the corrupted parties' input  $L \subseteq \{A, B, C\}$ . Then S sends  $\mathsf{op} = \mathsf{Output}$  and L to the ideal functionality, A, and  $A^{\mathsf{SH}}$ .

{58}------------------------------------------------

- (b) For each  $P \in L$ , S does the following:
  - i. (The parties in  $\pi_{\text{Output}}$  invoke  $\mathcal{F}_{\text{input}}$ ).  $\mathcal{A}$  outputs the corrupted parties' shares  $\{\mathbf{0}\}_{\mathcal{C}}$ .
  - ii. S receives (inst<sub>P</sub>,  $w_P$ ) from the ideal functionality.
  - iii. S simulates the opening of  $[w_P]'$  using the **Open** operation and obtains  $w_P'$ .
  - iv. S sends (inst<sub>P</sub>,  $w'_P$ ) to the functionality.
- 5. When  $\mathcal{A}$  outputs a final message and terminates,  $\mathcal{S}$  outputs whatever  $\mathcal{A}$  outputs and then terminates.

## Simulating the Adversary's View Before Output

Before Output is invoked, the only messages received by  $\mathcal{A}$  are random values, such as  $x, u, \mathbf{W}^{\text{plaintext}}$ , or hiding commitments to various values, including  $\{\overline{E}\}_{\mathcal{H}}, \{\overline{W}\}_{\mathcal{H}} \{\overline{T}\}_{\mathcal{H}}, \mathbf{h}'_{\mathcal{H}}$ .  $\mathcal{S}$  correctly simulates these values by sampling random values or committing to arbitrary values. Furthermore, all of the honest parties' outputs are the values inst', and possibly  $\pi'$ , that are computed by each operation.  $\mathcal{S}$  correctly simulates these outputs by simulating the reconstruction procedure.

<span id="page-58-0"></span>**Lemma 32.** At the end of any operation  $op \in \{Input, Fold, Increment, ZK, Rand\}$ , before Output is invoked, the messages received by A up to this point are identically distributed in the real and ideal worlds.

*Proof.* First, during initialization, the real-world  $\mathcal{A}$  receives as input  $\mathsf{pk}_{\mathsf{NIFS}}$  and a uniformly random tape r. In the ideal world,  $\mathcal{S}$  initializes  $\mathcal{A}$  on these values as well.

Next, let us consider each operation  $op \in \{Input, Fold, Increment, ZK, Rand\}$  that could be executed before Output and show that S correctly simulates A's view during this operation.

**During op** = Input: The real-world adversary receives its external inputs (inst, w, P) but does not receive any other messages. Even though the parties invoke  $\mathcal{F}_{input}$ , this functionality does not send any outputs to the corrupted parties. Likewise, the ideal-world adversary receives its external inputs (inst, w, P) from  $\mathcal{S}$  and does not receive any other messages.

**During op** = Fold: The real-world adversary (Section 5.1.2) receives its external inputs (P,Q) and then receives  $\{\overline{T}\}_{\mathcal{H}}$ .  $\{\overline{T}\}_{\mathcal{H}}$  is uniformly random over  $\mathbb{G}^{|\mathcal{H}|}$  because each honest party  $j \in \mathcal{H}$  samples  $\{r_{\mathbf{T}}\}_j$  uniformly at random and computes

$$\left\{\overline{T}\right\}_{j} = \mathsf{Ped.Commit}(\mathsf{pp}_{E}, \{\mathbf{T}\}_{j}; \{r_{\mathbf{T}}\}_{j})$$

Likewise in the ideal world, S sends (P,Q) to A, and during the **Open** operation, S sends  $\{\overline{T}\}_{\mathcal{H}}$  to A. Furthermore,  $\{\overline{T}\}_{\mathcal{H}}$  has the same distribution as it did in the real world. First, S receives  $\pi = \overline{T}$  from the ideal functionality (Fig. 4.6).  $\overline{T}$  is computed using Nova's folding algorithm NIFS. $\mathcal{P}_1$ , which samples  $r_{\mathbf{T}}$  uniformly and computes  $\overline{T} = \mathsf{Ped.Commit}(\mathsf{pp}_E, \mathbf{T}; r_{\mathbf{T}})$ . Then  $\overline{T}$  is uniformly random due to the randomness of  $r_{\mathbf{T}}$ . Second, S samples  $\{\overline{T}\}_{\mathcal{H}}$  uniformly at random such that  $\overline{T} = \mathsf{SSS.open}\left(\mathbb{G}, (\{\overline{T}\}_{\mathcal{H}}, \{\overline{T}\}_{\mathcal{C}}^{\mathsf{SH}})\right)$ . Since  $\overline{T}$  is uniformly random,  $\{\overline{T}\}_{\mathcal{H}}$  is uniformly random over  $\mathbb{G}^{|\mathcal{H}|}$ .

**During op** = Increment: The real-world adversary (Section 5.1.3) receives external inputs  $(\ell, z_0, \vec{\omega}^{\mathcal{C}}, \mathsf{Inst}, \mathsf{inst}, \pi)$  as well as messages  $\mathbf{h}'_{\mathcal{H}}$  and  $\{\overline{W}\}_{\mathcal{H}}$ . Even though  $\pi_{\mathsf{Increment}}$  invokes  $\mathcal{F}_F$  and possibly  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{input}}$ , these functionalities do not send any messages to the corrupted parties. Furthermore,  $\mathbf{h}'_{\mathcal{H}}$  and  $\{\overline{W}\}_{\mathcal{H}}$  are uniformly random. This is because each honest party  $j \in \mathcal{H}$  samples  $r'_j$  and  $\{r_{\mathbf{W}}\}_j$  uniformly at random. Next, they compute:

$$\begin{split} h'_j &= \mathsf{HCom.Commit}(z'_j; r'_j) \\ \{\overline{W}\}_j &= \mathsf{Ped.Commit}(\mathsf{pp}_W, \{\mathbf{W}\}_j; \{r_{\mathbf{W}}\}_j) \end{split}$$

Then  $h'_j$  and  $\{\overline{W}\}_j$  are uniformly random due to the randomness of  $r'_j$  and  $\{r_{\mathbf{W}}\}_j$ , respectively. Additionally, the honest parties output inst  $= (\overline{E}, u, \overline{W}, \mathsf{x})$ , where

$$\begin{split} \overline{E} &= \mathsf{Ped.Commit}(\mathsf{pp}_E, \mathbf{0}^M; 0) \\ u &= 1 \\ \overline{W} &= \mathsf{SSS.open}(\mathbb{G}, \{\overline{W}\}) \\ \mathsf{x} &= \mathbf{h}' = (h_0', h_1', \dots, h_n') \end{split}$$

{59}------------------------------------------------

In the ideal world, S sends to A the external inputs  $(\ell, z_0, \vec{\omega}^{\mathcal{C}}, \mathsf{Inst}, \mathsf{inst}, \pi)$  as well as  $\mathbf{h}'_{\mathcal{H}}$  and  $\{\overline{W}\}_{\mathcal{H}}$ . Furthermore, the distribution of  $\mathbf{h}'_{\mathcal{H}}$  and  $\{\overline{W}\}_{\mathcal{H}}$  is the same as in the real world. First, the ideal functionality (in Fig. 4.7) samples  $r_{\mathbf{W}}$  and  $\mathbf{r}'_{\mathcal{H}}$  uniformly at random, then computes

$$h'_j = \mathsf{HCom.Commit}(z'_j; r'_j), \quad \forall j \in \mathcal{H}$$
  
 $\overline{W} = \mathsf{Ped.Commit}(\mathsf{pp}_W, \mathbf{W}; r_{\mathbf{W}})$ 

 $\overline{W}$  is uniformly random due to the randomness of  $r_{\mathbf{W}}$ , and  $\mathbf{h}'_{\mathcal{H}}$  is uniformly random due to the randomness of  $\mathbf{r}'_{\mathcal{H}}$ . Next,  $\mathcal{S}$  samples  $\{\overline{W}\}_{\mathcal{H}}$  uniformly at random such that  $\overline{W} = \mathsf{SSS.open}\left(\mathbb{G}, (\{\overline{W}\}_{\mathcal{H}}, \{\overline{W}\}_{\mathcal{C}}^{\mathsf{SH}})\right)$ . Since  $\overline{W}$  is uniformly random,  $\{\overline{W}\}_{\mathcal{H}}$  is also uniformly random.

**During op** = ZK: The real-world adversary receives external inputs  $(\ell, z_0, \mathsf{Inst}, \mathsf{inst}', \pi, \pi')$  as well as messages  $\mathbf{h}'_{\mathcal{H}}$  and  $\{\overline{W}\}_{\mathcal{H}}$ . In the ideal world,  $\mathcal{S}$  correctly simulates these messages. The proof of this fact is similar to the argument made above for  $\mathsf{op} = \mathsf{Increment}$ .

**During op** = Rand: The real-world adversary receives  $(\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}, \mathsf{x}, u)$  when the parties call  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{coin}}$  and receives  $\{\overline{E}\}_{\mathcal{H}}, \{\overline{W}\}_{\mathcal{H}}$  when the parties reconstruct  $\overline{E}, \overline{W}$ . Although the parties also call  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{rand}}$ , this functionality does not send any messages to the corrupted parties.

All of these values  $-\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}, \mathsf{x}, u, \{\overline{E}\}_{\mathcal{H}}, \{\overline{W}\}_{\mathcal{H}}$  – are uniformly random and independent. First,  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{coin}}$  samples  $(\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}, \mathsf{x}, u)$  uniformly and independently. Second, each honest party  $j \in \mathcal{H}$  samples  $\{r_{\mathbf{E}}\}_{j}, \{r_{\mathbf{W}}\}_{j}$  uniformly at random and computes:

$$\begin{split} &\{\overline{E}\}_j = \mathsf{Ped.Commit}(\mathsf{pp}_E, \{\mathbf{E}\}_j; \{r_{\mathbf{E}}\}_j) \\ &\{\overline{W}\}_j = \mathsf{Ped.Commit}(\mathsf{pp}_W, \{\mathbf{W}\}_j; \{r_{\mathbf{W}}\}_j) \end{split}$$

Then  $\{\overline{E}\}_{\mathcal{H}}, \{\overline{W}\}_{\mathcal{H}}$  are uniformly random due to the randomness of  $\{r_{\mathbf{E}}\}_{\mathcal{H}}, \{r_{\mathbf{W}}\}_{\mathcal{H}}$ , respectively.

In the ideal world, S simulates these messages correctly. First, S samples  $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}} \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}^{N^{\mathsf{plaintext}}}$  and sends  $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}$  to A. Second, the ideal functionality (in Fig. 4.9) samples  $(\mathsf{x},u) \stackrel{\$}{\leftarrow} \mathbb{F}^L \times \mathbb{F}$ , and S sends  $(\mathsf{x},u)$  to A. Third, S sends  $\{\overline{E}\}_{\mathcal{H}}, \{\overline{W}\}_{\mathcal{H}}$  to A during the **Open** procedure.

Finally, the values of  $\{\overline{E}\}_{\mathcal{H}}, \{\overline{W}\}_{\mathcal{H}}$  that  $\mathcal{S}$  sends to  $\mathcal{A}$  are uniformly random. First, the ideal functionality (Fig. 4.9) samples  $(r_{\mathbf{E}}, r_{\mathbf{W}})$  uniformly at random and computes:

$$\begin{split} \overline{E} &= \mathsf{Ped.Commit}(\mathsf{pp}_E, \mathbf{E}; r_{\mathbf{E}}), \\ \overline{W} &= \mathsf{Ped.Commit}(\mathsf{pp}_W, \mathbf{W}; r_{\mathbf{W}}) \end{split}$$

Then  $(\overline{E}, \overline{W})$  are uniformly random due to the randomness of  $(r_{\mathbf{E}}, r_{\mathbf{W}})$ . Second,  $\mathcal{S}$  samples  $\{\overline{E}\}_{\mathcal{H}}$  and  $\{\overline{W}\}_{\mathcal{H}}$  uniformly at random such that

$$\overline{E} = \mathsf{SSS.open}\left(\mathbb{G}, (\{\overline{E}\}_{\mathcal{H}}, \{\overline{E}\}_{\mathcal{C}}^{\mathsf{SH}})\right)$$

$$\overline{W} = \mathsf{SSS.open}\left(\mathbb{G}, (\{\overline{W}\}_{\mathcal{H}}, \{\overline{W}\}_{\mathcal{C}}^{\mathsf{SH}})\right)$$

Since  $(\overline{E}, \overline{W})$  are uniformly random, then  $(\{\overline{E}\}_{\mathcal{H}}, \{\overline{W}\}_{\mathcal{H}})$  are also uniformly random.

#### Correspondence Property

In this section, we will show that after any operation, the current running values of ( $inst_A$ ,  $w_A$ ,  $inst_B$ ,  $w_B$ ,  $inst_C$ ,  $w_C$ ) are the same in the real and ideal worlds.
