This is known as the *correspondence property*, and it allows us to prove the correctness of our protocol.

**Ideal-World Definitions.** In the ideal world, after any given operation, let us define the **running** state to be the values of (inst<sub>A</sub>, w<sub>A</sub>, inst<sub>B</sub>, w<sub>B</sub>, inst<sub>C</sub>, w<sub>C</sub>) that are stored by  $\mathcal{F}_{CFS}$ . Next, let the adversary's view be all the messages and inputs sent to  $\mathcal{S}$ 's simulation of  $\mathcal{A}$ .

{60}------------------------------------------------

The Semi-Honest Adversary in the Real World. In the real world, it is tricky to define the running state.  $w_A, w_B, w_C$  are secret shared among the parties rather than expressed in plaintext form. The corrupted parties may change their shares arbitrarily, and this changes the values of  $w_A, w_B, w_C$  that can be reconstructed. Furthermore, parts of  $[w_A], [w_B], [w_C]$  have sharing degree n-1, so the honest parties' shares do not uniquely determine the values of  $w_A, w_B, w_C$ .

To get around these difficulties, we will define a semi-honest adversary  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$  in the real world that stores the "correct" shares of the corrupted parties. Then the shares held by the honest parties and  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$  define the values of  $\mathsf{w}_A, \mathsf{w}_B, \mathsf{w}_C$ .

In the real world, the honest parties  $\mathcal{H}$  interact with the corrupted parties, controlled by  $\mathcal{A}$ . Additionally, let us introduce the following semi-honest adversary  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$  to the real world.  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$  sees all the messages sent to and from  $\mathcal{A}$  but does not send any messages itself, so  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$  does not influence the protocol run between  $\mathcal{H}$  and  $\mathcal{A}$ .  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$  runs the honest protocol on behalf of the corrupted parties, except for the cases listed below, which are designed to keep  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$  up to date when  $\mathcal{A}$  outputs incorrect values.

- 1. When the parties invoke  $\mathcal{F}_{input}$ ,  $\mathcal{F}_{rand}$ , or  $\mathcal{F}_{F}$  to generate a sharing [x],  $\mathcal{A}$  provides shares of the output  $[x]_{\mathcal{C}}$ . Then  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$  overwrites their value of  $[x]_{\mathcal{C}}$  with the one provided by  $\mathcal{A}$ .
- 2. When the parties invoke  $\mathcal{F}_F$ ,  $\mathcal{A}$  provides inputs  $(z_j, \omega^j)_{j \in \mathcal{C}}$ . Then  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$  overwrites its values of  $(z_j, \omega^j)_{j \in \mathcal{C}}$  with the ones provided by  $\mathcal{A}$ .
- 3. Whenever the parties reconstruct  $\overline{E}$ ,  $\overline{W}$ ,  $\overline{T}$ ,  $\mathbf{h}'$ ,  $\mathbf{w}_A$ ,  $\mathbf{w}_B$ , or  $\mathbf{w}_C$ , if  $\mathcal{A}$  sends incorrect shares, then  $\mathcal{H}$  and  $\mathcal{A}$  may reconstruct an incorrect value. In this case,  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$  overwrites its value of  $\overline{E}$ ,  $\overline{W}$ ,  $\overline{T}$ ,  $\mathbf{h}'$ ,  $\mathbf{w}_A$ ,  $\mathbf{w}_B$ , or  $\mathbf{w}_C$  with the value that  $\mathcal{H}$  and  $\mathcal{A}$  actually reconstructed.

**Real-World Definitions.** Now that we have defined  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$ , we can define  $(\mathsf{inst}_A, \mathsf{w}_A, \mathsf{inst}_B, \mathsf{w}_B, \mathsf{inst}_C, \mathsf{w}_C)$  in the real world. Let  $\mathsf{inst}_A, \mathsf{inst}_B$  and  $\mathsf{inst}_C$  be the values held by  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$ . Let  $[\mathsf{w}_A]_{\mathcal{C}}^{\mathsf{SH}}, [\mathsf{w}_B]_{\mathcal{C}}^{\mathsf{SH}}, [\mathsf{w}_C]_{\mathcal{C}}^{\mathsf{SH}}$  be the shares held by  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$ , and let  $[\mathsf{w}_A]_{\mathcal{H}}, [\mathsf{w}_B]_{\mathcal{H}}, [\mathsf{w}_C]_{\mathcal{H}}$  be the shares held by  $\mathcal{H}$ . Finally, let

$$\begin{aligned} \mathbf{w}_{A} &= \mathsf{SSS.open}(\mathbb{F}, ([\mathbf{w}_{A}]_{\mathcal{H}}, [\mathbf{w}_{A}]_{\mathcal{C}}^{\mathsf{SH}})) \\ \mathbf{w}_{B} &= \mathsf{SSS.open}(\mathbb{F}, ([\mathbf{w}_{B}]_{\mathcal{H}}, [\mathbf{w}_{B}]_{\mathcal{C}}^{\mathsf{SH}})) \\ \mathbf{w}_{C} &= \mathsf{SSS.open}(\mathbb{F}, ([\mathbf{w}_{C}]_{\mathcal{H}}, [\mathbf{w}_{C}]_{\mathcal{C}}^{\mathsf{SH}})) \end{aligned}$$

Finally, let the adversary's view be all messages and inputs sent to A.

**Correspondence Property.** We will prove the following correspondence property, which says that  $(inst_A, w_A, inst_B, w_B, inst_C, w_C)$  have the same distribution in the real and ideal worlds.

<span id="page-60-0"></span>**Definition 33 (Correspondence Property).** At a given time between operations, the distribution of the running state  $(\mathsf{inst}_A, \mathsf{w}_A, \mathsf{inst}_B, \mathsf{w}_B, \mathsf{inst}_C, \mathsf{w}_C)$ , conditioned on the adversary's view, is identically distributed in the real and ideal worlds.

We will show that the correspondence property is satisfied after any operation before Output.

<span id="page-60-1"></span>**Lemma 34.** At the end of any operation  $op \in \{Input, Fold, Increment, ZK, Rand\}$ , before Output is invoked, the correspondence property is satisfied.

*Proof.* We will prove this inductively.

First, the correspondence property is initially satisfied. In the ideal world,  $\mathcal{F}_{CFS}$  initializes its state as follows:

$$(\mathsf{inst}_A, \mathsf{w}_A) = (\mathsf{inst}_B, \mathsf{w}_B) = (\mathsf{inst}_C, \mathsf{w}_C) = (\mathsf{inst}_\bot, \mathsf{w}_\bot)$$

In the real world, each party  $j \in [n]$ , who is controlled by  $\mathcal{H}$  or  $\mathcal{A}^{SH}$ , initializes their state as follows:

$$(\mathsf{inst}_A, [\mathsf{w}_A]_i) = (\mathsf{inst}_B, [\mathsf{w}_B]_i) = (\mathsf{inst}_C, [\mathsf{w}_C]_i) = (\mathsf{inst}_\bot, \mathsf{w}_\bot)$$

Then the sharings  $[w_A]$ ,  $[w_B]$ ,  $[w_C]$  represent valid degree-0 sharings of  $w_{\perp}$ , so the correspondence property is satisfied by this initial state.

Next, we will consider any  $op \in \{Input, Fold, Increment, ZK, Rand\}$  and show that if the correspondence property is satisfied at the start of the operation, then it will still be satisfied at the end of the operation. See Lemmas 35, 36, 38, 39 and 41 for the proof of this fact.

{61}------------------------------------------------

**Operation Input:** Let us consider one execution of op = Input in the ideal world and compare it to the real-world execution of  $\pi_{Input}$ .

<span id="page-61-0"></span>**Lemma 35.** Given an execution of op = Input, if the correspondence property (Definition 33) is satisfied before the execution, then it will still be satisfied after the execution.

*Proof.* In the ideal world (Fig. 4.5), this operation takes inputs (inst, w, P) and writes (inst, w) to position P:

$$(\mathsf{inst}_P, \mathsf{w}_P) \leftarrow (\mathsf{inst}, \mathsf{w})$$

In the real world (Section 5.1.1), the parties have inputs (inst, w, P). The only messages that  $\mathcal{A}$  sends during  $\pi_{\text{identity}}$  are the shares ( $[\mathbf{E}^{\text{shared}}]_{\mathcal{C}}$ ,  $[\mathbf{W}^{\text{shared}}]_{\mathcal{C}}$ ,  $[r_{\mathbf{E}}]_{\mathcal{C}}$ ,  $[r_{\mathbf{W}}]_{\mathcal{C}}$ ) that they send to  $\mathcal{F}_{\text{input}}$ .  $\mathcal{A}^{\text{SH}}$  sets its shares of ( $\mathbf{E}^{\text{shared}}$ ,  $\mathbf{W}^{\text{shared}}$ ,  $r_{\mathbf{E}}$ ,  $r_{\mathbf{W}}$ ) to be the ones that  $\mathcal{A}$  sent to  $\mathcal{F}_{\text{input}}$ . Next,  $\mathcal{F}_{\text{input}}$  generates a sharing of w that is consistent with  $\mathcal{A}$ 's shares. At the end of the protocol  $\mathcal{H}$  and  $\mathcal{A}^{\text{SH}}$  hold shares [w] that satisfy  $\mathbf{w} = \mathsf{SSS.open}(\mathbb{F}, [\mathbf{w}])$ . Therefore, the correspondence property holds at the end of this execution.

**Operation Rand:** Let us consider one execution of op = Rand in the ideal world and compare it to the real-world execution of  $\pi_{Rand}$ .

<span id="page-61-1"></span>**Lemma 36.** Given an execution of op = Rand, if the correspondence property (Definition 33) is satisfied before the execution, then it will still be satisfied after the execution.

*Proof.* This operation computes an instance inst =  $(\overline{E}, u, \overline{W}, x)$  and a witness  $w = (\mathbf{E}, r_{\mathbf{E}}, \mathbf{W}, r_{\mathbf{W}})$ . We will show that these variables have the same distribution in the ideal and real worlds.

First, let us consider  $(x, u, r_{\mathbf{W}}, r_{\mathbf{E}})$ . In the ideal world (Fig. 4.9), the variables  $x, u, r_{\mathbf{W}}, r_{\mathbf{E}}$  are sampled uniformly at random. In the real world (Section 5.1.5),  $\mathcal{F}_{\text{coin}}$  samples x, u uniformly at random. Additionally, the parties sample the shares  $\{r_{\mathbf{W}}\}, \{r_{\mathbf{E}}\}$  uniformly at random. They represent degree-(n-1) sharings of uniformly random values  $(r_{\mathbf{W}}, r_{\mathbf{E}})$ .

Second, let us consider  $\mathbf{W}$ . In the ideal world,  $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}$  is sampled uniformly by  $\mathcal{S}$  and provided as input to the functionality's Rand operation. Next,  $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{shared}}$ ,  $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{mixed}}$ ,  $(\mathbf{W}^j)_{j\in\mathcal{H}}$  are sampled uniformly and independently at random. Finally  $(\mathbf{W}^j)_{j\in\mathcal{C}}$  are supplied to the functionality by  $\mathcal{S}$ 's simulation of  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$ .

In the real world,  $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}$  is sampled uniformly by  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{coin}}$ . The protocol also calls  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{rand}}$  to generate  $[\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{shared}}]$ ,  $[\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{mixed}}]$ , with  $\mathcal{H}$  holding  $[\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{shared}}]_{\mathcal{H}}$ ,  $[\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{mixed}}]_{\mathcal{H}}$  and  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$  holding  $[\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{shared}}]_{\mathcal{C}}$ . These shares correspond to uniformly random vectors  $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{shared}}$ ,  $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{mixed}}$ . Additionally, the honest parties sample  $(\mathbf{W}^j)_{j\in\mathcal{H}}$  uniformly at random, and  $(\mathbf{W}^j)_{j\in\mathcal{C}}$  are sampled from  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$ 's random tape r, which is the same tape used to sample  $(\mathbf{W}^j)_{j\in\mathcal{C}}$  in the ideal world.

Third, let us consider  $\mathbf{E}$ . In the ideal world,  $\mathbf{E}$  is uniquely determined by  $\mathbf{W}, \mathsf{x}, u$ . Recall that  $\mathbf{Z} = (\mathbf{W}, \mathsf{x}, u)$ . Then

<span id="page-61-2"></span>
$$\mathbf{E} = (\mathbf{A} \cdot \mathbf{Z}) \circ (\mathbf{B} \cdot \mathbf{Z}) - u \cdot (\mathbf{C} \cdot \mathbf{Z}) \tag{5.2}$$

Lemma 37 shows that in the real world, **E** is also given by Eq. (5.2).

Next, we will consider  $\overline{E}$  in the case where the adversary is semi-honest. In the ideal world,  $\overline{E}$  is uniquely determined by  $\mathbf{E}, r_{\mathbf{E}}$  as follows:

$$\overline{E} = \text{Ped.Commit}(pp_E, \mathbf{E}; r_{\mathbf{E}})$$
 (5.3)

<span id="page-61-3"></span>In the real world,  $\mathbf{E}, r_{\mathbf{E}}, \overline{E}$  also satisfy Eq. (5.3). Ped.Commit is additively homomorphic, so

$$\begin{split} \overline{E} &= \mathsf{SSS.open}(\mathbb{G}, \{\overline{E}\}) \\ &= \sum_{j \in [n]} L_j(0) \cdot \{\overline{E}\}_j \\ &= \sum_{j \in [n]} L_j(0) \cdot \mathsf{Ped.Commit}(\mathsf{pp}_E, \{\mathbf{E}\}_j; \{r_{\mathbf{E}}\}_j) \\ &= \mathsf{Ped.Commit}\left(\mathsf{pp}_E, \sum_{j \in [n]} L_j(0) \cdot \{\mathbf{E}\}_j; \sum_{j \in [n]} L_j(0) \cdot \{r_{\mathbf{E}}\}_j\right) \\ &= \mathsf{Ped.Commit}\left(\mathsf{pp}_E, \mathbf{E}; r_{\mathbf{E}}\right) \end{split}$$

So  $\{\overline{E}\}\$  is a valid sharing of Ped.Commit( $pp_E, \mathbf{E}; r_{\mathbf{E}}$ ). Likewise,  $\{\overline{W}\}\$  is a valid sharing of Ped.Commit( $pp_W, \mathbf{W}; r_{\mathbf{W}}$ ).

{62}------------------------------------------------

Let us consider  $\overline{E}$  in the case where the adversary is malicious. In the real world,  $\overline{E}$  is reconstructed after the parties publish their shares of  $\{\overline{E}\}$ . First, the adversary's view defines  $\{\overline{E}\}_{\mathcal{C}}^{\mathsf{SH}}$ , the shares that they would output if they were honest. Second, the honest parties publish their shares  $\{\overline{E}\}_{\mathcal{H}}$ , which are uniformly random, due to the randomness of  $\{r_{\mathbf{E}}\}_{\mathcal{H}}$ , over all shares such that  $\mathsf{SSS.open}(\mathbb{G}, \{\overline{E}\}_{\mathcal{H}}, \{\overline{E}\}_{\mathcal{C}}^{\mathsf{SH}}) = \mathsf{Ped.Commit}(\mathsf{pp}_E, \mathbf{E}; r_{\mathbf{E}})$ . Finally, the corrupted parties output  $\{\overline{E}\}_{\mathcal{C}}$ , which might be different from  $\{\overline{E}\}_{\mathcal{C}}^{\mathsf{SH}}$ , and the parties reconstruct  $\overline{E} = \mathsf{SSS.open}(\mathbb{F}, \{\overline{E}\}_{\mathcal{H}}, \{\overline{E}\}_{\mathcal{C}})$ . In the ideal world,  $\mathsf{Open}$  correctly simulates the real-world protocol for opening  $\{\overline{E}\}$ , so the value of  $\overline{E}$  that is reconstructed will be the same in the real and ideal worlds. Then  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{CFS}}$  overwrites the value of  $\overline{E}$  with whatever value the parties reconstruct.

Finally, the analysis of  $\overline{W}$  is the same as that of  $\overline{E}$ , so  $\overline{W}$  has the same distribution in the real and ideal worlds.

In summary, we've shown that all variables that make up (inst, w) have the same distribution in the real and ideal worlds given the adversary's view.

<span id="page-62-0"></span>**Lemma 37.** In the real-world execution of  $\pi_{\mathsf{Rand}}$  (Section 5.1.5), the shares  $\{\mathbf{W}\}, \{\mathbf{E}\}$  define values of  $\mathbf{W}, \mathbf{Z}, \mathbf{E}$  that satisfy  $\mathbf{E} = (\mathbf{A} \cdot \mathbf{Z}) \circ (\mathbf{B} \cdot \mathbf{Z}) - u \cdot (\mathbf{C} \cdot \mathbf{Z})$ .

*Proof.* Each party  $j \in [n]$  computes the following:

$$\begin{aligned} \{\mathbf{Z}\}_j &= \left(\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}, [\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{shared}}]_j, [\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{mixed}}]_j, L_j(0) \cdot \left(\{\mathbf{W}^{j'}\}_j\right)_{j' \in [n]}, \mathsf{x}, u\right) \\ \{\mathbf{E}\}_j &= \mathbf{A} \cdot \{\mathbf{Z}\}_j \circ \mathbf{B} \cdot \{\mathbf{Z}\}_j - u \cdot \mathbf{C} \cdot \{\mathbf{Z}\}_j \end{aligned}$$

Then for each  $j' \in [n]$ , party j overwrites  $\{\mathbf{E}^{j'}\}_{j}$ :

$$\{\mathbf{E}^{j'}\}_j \leftarrow \frac{\mathbb{1}_{j'=j}}{L_j(0)} \cdot \{\mathbf{E}^{j'}\}_j$$

Let us analyze each component of  $\{E\}$ . For each subcircuit  $\in \{\text{plaintext}, \text{shared}, \text{mixed}\},$ 

$$\{\mathbf{E}^{\mathsf{subcircuit}}\} = \mathbf{A}^{\mathsf{subcircuit}} \cdot \{\mathbf{Z}\} \circ \mathbf{B}^{\mathsf{subcircuit}} \cdot \{\mathbf{Z}\} - u \cdot \mathbf{C}^{\mathsf{subcircuit}} \cdot \{\mathbf{Z}\}$$

This procedure computes  $\mathbf{E}^{\text{subcircuit}}$  homomorphically.  $\{\mathbf{Z}\}$  is a Shamir sharing, so  $\{\mathbf{E}^{\text{subcircuit}}\}$  represents n evaluations of a polynomial whose y-intercept is  $\mathbf{E}^{\text{subcircuit}}$ .  $\{\mathbf{E}^{\text{subcircuit}}\}$  is a valid sharing of  $\mathbf{E}^{\text{subcircuit}}$  as long as the degree of the sharing polynomial is  $\leq n-1$ .

First,  $\mathbf{E}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}$  corresponds to the circuit  $H^{\mathsf{plaintext}}$ , which only uses wire values in  $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}$ .  $\{\mathbf{Z}\}$  represents  $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}$  with a Shamir sharing of degree 0. Therefore  $\{\mathbf{E}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}\}$  is also a Shamir sharing of degree  $0 \le n-1$  of the correct value of  $\mathbf{E}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}$ .

Second,  $\mathbf{E}^{\mathsf{shared}}$  corresponds to the circuit  $H^{\mathsf{shared}}$ , which only uses wire values in  $(\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}, \mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{shared}})$ .  $\{\mathbf{Z}\}$  represents  $(\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}, \mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{shared}})$  with Shamir sharings of degrees  $\leq t$ . Therefore  $\{\mathbf{E}^{\mathsf{shared}}\}$  is a Shamir sharing of degree  $\leq 2t \leq n-1$  of the correct value of  $\mathbf{E}^{\mathsf{shared}}$ .

Third,  $\mathbf{E}^{\mathsf{mixed}}$  corresponds to the circuit  $H^{\mathsf{mixed}}$ , which computes a linear function. That means  $\mathbf{B}^{\mathsf{mixed}} = \mathbf{0}$ , so

$$\{\mathbf{E}^{\mathsf{mixed}}\} = -u \cdot \mathbf{C}^{\mathsf{mixed}} \cdot \{\mathbf{Z}\}$$

 $\{\mathbf{E}^{\mathsf{mixed}}\}\$  is obtained by applying a linear function to  $\{\mathbf{Z}\}$ . Since  $\{\mathbf{Z}\}$  is a Shamir sharing of degree  $\leq n-1$ ,  $\{\mathbf{E}^{\mathsf{mixed}}\}$  is a Shamir sharing of degree  $\leq n-1$  of the correct value of  $\mathbf{E}^{\mathsf{mixed}}$ .

Fourth, for each  $j \in [n]$ ,  $\mathbf{E}^j$  corresponds to the circuit  $H^j$ , which only uses wire values in  $(\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}, \mathbf{W}^j, \mathsf{x})$ .  $\{\mathbf{Z}\}_j$  represents  $(\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}, \mathsf{x})$  in plaintext form. Furthermore, the component of  $\{\mathbf{Z}\}_j$  corresponding to  $\mathbf{W}^j$  is

$$L_j(0) \cdot \{\mathbf{W}^j\}_j = \frac{L_j(0)}{L_j(0)} \cdot \mathbf{W}^j = \mathbf{W}^j$$

So  $\{\mathbf{Z}\}_j$  also represents  $\mathbf{W}^j$  in plaintext form. Then party j computes  $\{\mathbf{E}^j\}_j = \mathbf{E}^j$  in plaintext form:

$$\begin{aligned} \{\mathbf{E}^j\}_j &= \mathbf{A}^j \cdot \{\mathbf{Z}\}_j \circ \mathbf{B}^j \cdot \{\mathbf{Z}\}_j - u \cdot \mathbf{C}^j \cdot \{\mathbf{Z}\}_j \\ \{\mathbf{E}^j\}_j &= \mathbf{A}^j \cdot \mathbf{Z} \circ \mathbf{B}^j \cdot \mathbf{Z} - u \cdot \mathbf{C}^j \cdot \mathbf{Z} \\ &= \mathbf{E}^j \end{aligned}$$

Finally, party j overwrites  $\{\mathbf{E}^j\}_j$  as follows:

$$\{\mathbf{E}^j\}_j \leftarrow \frac{1}{L_j(0)} \cdot \mathbf{E}^j$$

{63}------------------------------------------------

And for any  $j' \neq j$ , party j sets

$$\{\mathbf{E}^{j'}\}_j \leftarrow \mathbf{0}^{M^{j'}}$$

In total,

$$\{\mathbf{E}^j\} = \operatorname{slice}(\mathbf{E}^j, M^j, j)$$

so  $\{\mathbf{E}^j\}$  is a valid sharing of the correct value  $\mathbf{E}^j$ .

In summary, we've shown that

$$\{\mathbf{E}\} = (\{\mathbf{E}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}\}, \{\mathbf{E}^{\mathsf{shared}}\}, \{\mathbf{E}^{\mathsf{mixed}}\}, \{\mathbf{E}^1\}, \dots, \{\mathbf{E}^n\})$$

is a valid Shamir sharing of degree  $\leq n-1$  of the correct value of **E**.

<span id="page-63-0"></span>**Operation Fold:** Let us compare an execution of op = Fold in the real and ideal worlds.

**Lemma 38.** Given an execution of op = Fold, if the correspondence property (Definition 33) is satisfied before the execution, then it will still be satisfied after the execution.

*Proof.* This operation computes a folded instance inst =  $(\overline{E}, u, \overline{W}, x)$ , a folded witness  $w = (\mathbf{E}, r_{\mathbf{E}}, \mathbf{W}, r_{\mathbf{W}})$ , and a folding proof  $\pi' = \overline{T}$ . We will show that these variables have the same distribution in the real and ideal worlds.

First let us consider the distribution of **T**. In the ideal world (Fig. 4.6), Nova's folding algorithm NIFS. $\mathcal{P}_1$  computes

$$\mathbf{T} = \mathbf{A} \cdot \mathbf{Z}_P \circ \mathbf{B} \cdot \mathbf{Z}_Q + \mathbf{A} \cdot \mathbf{Z}_Q \circ \mathbf{B} \cdot \mathbf{Z}_P - u_P \cdot \mathbf{C} \cdot \mathbf{Z}_Q - u_Q \cdot \mathbf{C} \cdot \mathbf{Z}_P$$

The real-world protocol  $\pi_{\mathsf{Fold}}$  (Section 5.1.2) computes shares  $\{\mathbf{T}\}$  that satisfy  $\mathbf{T} = \mathsf{SSS.open}(\mathbb{F}, \{\mathbf{T}\})$ . The proof of this fact is essentially the same as the proof of Lemma 37.

Second, let us consider the distribution of  $r_{\mathbf{T}}$ . In the ideal world,  $r_{\mathbf{T}}$  is sampled uniformly at random. In the real world, the parties sample the shares  $\{r_{\mathbf{T}}\}$  uniformly at random. They represent a degree-(n-1) sharing of a uniformly random value  $r_{\mathbf{T}}$ .

Third, let us consider the distribution of  $\overline{T}$ . If the adversary is semi-honest, then the real and ideal worlds compute

$$\overline{T} = \mathsf{Ped.Commit}(\mathsf{pp}_E, \mathbf{T}; r_{\mathbf{T}})$$

If the adversary is malicious, then the real and ideal worlds set  $\overline{T}$  to be whatever value the parties reconstruct after publishing their shares  $\{\overline{T}\}$ . The proof of this follows the argument that was made for  $\overline{E}$  in the proof of Lemma 36.

Fourth, it is straightforward to verify that the real and ideal worlds compute inst from  $(\mathsf{vk}_{\mathsf{NIFS}},\mathsf{inst}_P,\mathsf{inst}_Q,\overline{T})$  according to  $\mathsf{NIFS}.\mathcal{P}_2$ 's procedure.

Fifth, let us consider the distribution of the folded witness w. In the ideal world, w is computed as follows.

$$\begin{split} (\mathbf{E}, r_{\mathbf{E}}) &= (\mathbf{E}_P, r_{\mathbf{E}_P}) + r \cdot (\mathbf{T}, r_{\mathbf{T}}) + r^2 \cdot \left(\mathbf{E}_Q, r_{\mathbf{E}_Q}\right) \\ (\mathbf{W}, r_{\mathbf{W}}) &= (\mathbf{W}_P, r_{\mathbf{W}_P}) + r \cdot \left(\mathbf{W}_Q, r_{\mathbf{W}_Q}\right) \\ \mathbf{w} &= (\mathbf{E}, r_{\mathbf{E}}, \mathbf{W}, r_{\mathbf{W}}) \end{split}$$

The real world computes the same linear function homomorphically on shares  $\{\mathbf{E}_P\}$ ,  $\{r_{\mathbf{E}_P}\}$ ,  $\{\mathbf{W}_P\}$ ,  $\{r_{\mathbf{W}_P}\}$ ,  $\{\mathbf{E}_Q\}$ ,  $\{r_{\mathbf{E}_Q}\}$ ,  $\{r_{\mathbf{W}_Q}\}$ ,  $\{r_{\mathbf{W}_Q}\}$ ,  $\{r_{\mathbf{T}}\}$ . Therefore the real-world shares [w] satisfy  $\mathbf{w} = \mathsf{SSS.open}(\mathbb{F}, [\mathsf{w}])$ . This shows that at the end of the  $\mathsf{op} = \mathsf{Fold}$  operation, the correspondence property remains true.  $\square$ 

**Operation Increment:** Let us consider one execution of op = Increment in the ideal world and compare it to the real-world execution of  $\pi_{Increment}$ .

<span id="page-63-1"></span>**Lemma 39.** Given an execution of op = Increment, if the correspondence property (Definition 33) is satisfied before the execution, then it will still be satisfied after the execution.

{64}------------------------------------------------

*Proof.* This operation computes an instance inst =  $(\overline{E}, u, \overline{W}, x)$  and a witness  $w = (\mathbf{E}, r_{\mathbf{E}}, \mathbf{W}, r_{\mathbf{W}})$ . We will show that these variables have the same distribution in the ideal and real worlds.

First, both the real-world protocol (Section 5.1.3) and ideal-world protocol (Fig. 4.7) compute

$$(\mathbf{E}, r_{\mathbf{E}}, u) = (\mathbf{0}^{M}, 0, 1)$$

$$\overline{E} = \mathsf{Ped.Commit}(\mathsf{pp}_{E}, \mathbf{E}; r_{\mathbf{E}})$$

Second, the real-world protocol computes shares  $\{\mathbf{W}\}$ , and the ideal-world functionality computes  $\mathbf{W}$  such that  $\mathbf{W}$  and  $\mathsf{SSS.open}(\mathbb{F}, \{\mathbf{W}\})$  are identically distributed, given the adversary's view (Lemma 40).

Third, let us consider the distribution of x. If the adversary is semi-honest, then in the real and ideal worlds, x equals the value of  $\mathbf{h}'$  that is determined by  $\mathbf{W}$ . If the adversary is malicious, then in the real world, the adversary may send incorrect values  $\mathbf{h}'_{\mathcal{C}}$  rather than the correct values  $\mathbf{h}'_{\mathcal{C}}$ . In this case, the real world protocol will set  $\mathbf{x} = (h'_0, \mathbf{h}'_{\mathcal{H}}, \mathbf{h}''_{\mathcal{C}})$ . In the ideal world,  $\mathcal{S}$  extracts  $\mathbf{h}''_{\mathcal{C}}$  from  $\mathcal{A}$  and overwrites the value of x with  $(h'_0, \mathbf{h}'_{\mathcal{H}}, \mathbf{h}''_{\mathcal{C}})$ . Therefore, x has the same distribution in the real and ideal worlds.

Fourth, in the ideal world, Increment samples  $r_{\mathbf{W}}$  uniformly at random. In the real world, during  $\pi_{\mathsf{Increment}}$ , the parties sample  $\{r_{\mathbf{W}}\}$  uniformly at random. This represents a degree-(n-1) sharing of a uniformly random value  $r_{\mathbf{W}}$ .

Fifth, let us consider the distribution of  $\overline{W}$ . If the adversary is semi-honest, then the real and ideal worlds compute

$$\overline{W} = \mathsf{Ped.Commit}(\mathsf{pp}_W, \mathbf{W}; r_{\mathbf{W}})$$

If the adversary is malicious, then the real and ideal worlds set  $\overline{W}$  to be whatever value the parties reconstruct after publishing their shares  $\{\overline{W}\}$ . The proof of this follows the argument that was made for  $\overline{E}$  in the proof of Lemma 36.

In summary, we've shown that  $\mathsf{inst} = (\overline{E}, u, \overline{W}, \mathsf{x})$  and  $\mathsf{w} = (\mathbf{E}, r_{\mathbf{E}}, \mathbf{W}, r_{\mathbf{W}})$  have the same distribution in the real and ideal worlds given the adversary's view.

<span id="page-64-0"></span>**Lemma 40.** Given an execution of op = Increment, the real-world protocol computes shares  $\{\mathbf{W}\}$ , and the ideal-world functionality computes  $\mathbf{W}$  such that  $\mathsf{SSS.open}(\mathbb{F}, \{\mathbf{W}\})$  and  $\mathbf{W}$  are identically distributed given the adversary's view.

*Proof.* In the ideal world, Increment (Fig. 4.7) computes  $\mathbf{W}$  to be all the input and intermediate wire values of

$$H_F(\mathsf{vk}_{\mathsf{NIFS}},\mathsf{mode},\mathsf{mode}',\ell,z_0,z,\vec{\omega},\mathsf{inst}_\perp,\mathsf{Inst},\mathsf{inst}',\pi,\pi',\mathbf{h},\mathbf{z},\mathbf{z}',\mathbf{r},\mathbf{r}')$$

Here is how the functionality chooses the inputs to  $H_F$ .  $(\ell, z_0, \mathsf{Inst}, \mathsf{inst}, \pi)$  are provided as inputs to  $\mathcal{S}$ .  $\vec{\omega}^{\mathcal{H}}$  are provided as inputs to the honest parties.  $(\vec{\omega}^{\mathcal{C}}, [z_0]_{\mathcal{C}}, \mathbf{z}'_{\mathcal{C}})$  are obtained when  $\mathcal{S}$ 's simulation of  $\mathcal{A}$  provides them as inputs to  $\mathcal{F}_{input}$  or  $\mathcal{F}_F$ .  $\mathbf{r}'_{\mathcal{C}}$  is chosen by  $\mathcal{S}$ 's simulation of  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$ . The functionality computes  $(\mathsf{vk}_{\mathsf{NIFS}}, \mathsf{inst}_{\perp})$  from  $\mathsf{pk}_{\mathsf{NIFS}}$ , which was given to the functionality during initialization. If  $\ell \geq 1$ , then  $(\mathsf{mode}, z, \mathbf{z}, \mathbf{h}, \mathbf{r})$  are read from the state variables as follows:

$$(\mathsf{mode}, z, \mathbf{z}, \mathbf{h}, \mathbf{r}) \leftarrow (\mathsf{inst}_B, \mathsf{w}_B).(\mathsf{mode}', z', \mathbf{z}', \mathbf{h}', \mathbf{r}')$$

If  $\ell = 0$ , then (mode,  $z, \mathbf{z}, \mathbf{h}, \mathbf{r}$ ) are computed from the inputs  $(z_0, [z_0]_{\mathcal{C}})$  as follows:

$$(\mathsf{mode}, z, \mathbf{z}, \mathbf{h}, \mathbf{r}) = (\mathsf{increment}, z_0, \mathsf{SSS}.\mathsf{Share}(\mathbb{F}, z_0, [z_0]_{\mathcal{C}}, \mathcal{C}, t), \mathbf{0}^{n+1}, \mathbf{0}^n)$$

Next, the functionality chooses (mode', inst',  $\pi'$ ) = (increment, inst<sub> $\perp$ </sub>, 0). The functionality computes  $\mathbf{z}'$  from  $(z, \vec{\omega}, \mathbf{z}'_{\mathcal{C}})$  as follows:

$$\mathbf{z}' = \mathsf{SSS.Share}(\mathbb{F}, F(z, \vec{\omega}), \mathbf{z}'_{\mathcal{C}}, \mathcal{C}, t)$$

Finally the functionality samples  $\mathbf{r}_{\mathcal{H}}'$  uniformly at random.

In the real world (Section 5.1.3),  $\mathcal{H}$ ,  $\mathcal{A}$ , and  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$  compute the inputs of  $H_F$ , and the inputs have the same distribution as they do in the ideal world.  $(\ell, z_0, \mathsf{Inst}, \mathsf{inst}, \pi)$  are provided as inputs to  $\mathcal{H}$  and  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$ .  $\vec{\omega}^{\mathcal{H}}$  are provided as inputs to  $\mathcal{H}$ .  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$  obtains  $(\vec{\omega}^{\mathcal{C}}, [z_0]_{\mathcal{C}}, \mathbf{z}'_{\mathcal{C}})$  when  $\mathcal{A}$  provides them as inputs to  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{input}}$  or  $\mathcal{F}_F$ .  $\mathbf{r}'_{\mathcal{C}}$  is chosen by  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$ .  $\mathcal{H}$  and  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$  compute  $(\mathsf{vk}_{\mathsf{NIFS}}, \mathsf{inst}_{\perp})$  from  $\mathsf{pk}_{\mathsf{NIFS}}$ , which was given to them during initialization. If  $\ell \geq 1$ , then  $(\mathsf{mode}, [z], \mathbf{z}, \mathbf{h}, \mathbf{r})$  are read from the state variables as follows:

$$(\mathsf{mode}, [z], \mathbf{z}, \mathbf{h}, \mathbf{r}) \leftarrow (\mathsf{inst}_B, [\mathsf{w}_B]).(\mathsf{mode}', \mathbf{z}', \mathbf{z}', \mathbf{h}', \mathbf{r}')$$

{65}------------------------------------------------

Note that  $[z] = [w_B].\mathbf{z}'$ , and in the ideal world,  $z = w_B.z'$ . This ensures that SSS.open( $\mathbb{F}, [z]$ ) equals the ideal-world value of z because  $[w_B].\mathbf{z}'$  represents a valid sharing of  $w_B.z'$ .

If  $\ell = 0$ , then (mode,  $[z], \mathbf{z}, \mathbf{h}, \mathbf{r}$ ) are computed from the inputs  $(z_0, [z_0]_{\mathcal{C}})$  as follows:

(mode, 
$$\mathbf{z}, \mathbf{h}, \mathbf{r}$$
) = (increment, SSS.Share( $\mathbb{F}, z_0, [z_0]_{\mathcal{C}}, \mathcal{C}, t$ ),  $\mathbf{0}^{n+1}, \mathbf{0}^n$ )
$$[z] = \mathbf{z}$$

Note that SSS.open( $\mathbb{F},[z]$ ) =  $z_0$ , which is the ideal-world value of z.

Next,  $\mathcal{H}$  and  $\mathcal{A}^{SH}$  choose (mode', inst',  $\pi'$ ) = (increment, inst<sub>\(\pm\)</sub>, 0). The parties compute  $\mathbf{z}'$  by calling  $\mathcal{F}_F$  with inputs that include ([z],  $\vec{\omega}$ ,  $\mathbf{z}'_{\mathcal{C}}$ ). Then  $\mathcal{F}_F$  computes  $\mathbf{z}'$  as follows:

$$z = \mathsf{SSS.open}(\mathbb{F},[z])$$
  
 $\mathbf{z}' = \mathsf{SSS.Share}(\mathbb{F},F(z,\vec{\omega}),\mathbf{z}'_{\mathcal{C}},\mathcal{C},t)$ 

Then  $\mathbf{z}'$  has the same distribution as in the ideal world. Finally  $\mathcal{H}$  samples  $\mathbf{r}'_{\mathcal{H}}$  uniformly at random. In summary, we have listed all the inputs to  $H_F$  and shown that they have the same distribution in the real and ideal worlds.

Now we will show that in the real-world, the parties  $\mathcal{H}$  and  $\mathcal{A}^{SH}$  compute a sharing  $\{\mathbf{W}\}$  such that  $\mathsf{SSS.open}(\mathbb{F}, \{\mathbf{W}\})$  matches the ideal-world variable  $\mathbf{W}$ .

First, the parties compute [ $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{shared}}$ ] using  $\mathcal{F}_F$ . They provide inputs [z] and  $\vec{\omega}$ . Recall that  $z = \mathsf{SSS.open}(\mathbb{F},[z])$  and  $\vec{\omega}$  have the same distribution as they do in the ideal world. Then  $\mathcal{F}_F$  computes  $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{shared}}$  as it is computed in the ideal world, and sends a valid sharing [ $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{shared}}$ ] to the parties.

Second,  $\mathcal{H}$  and  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$  compute  $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}$  the same way it is computed in the ideal world, by evaluating  $H^{\mathsf{plaintext}}$ . Then  $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{plaintext}}$  is stored as a degree-0 sharing.

Third,  $\mathcal{H}$  computes  $(\mathbf{W}^j)_{j\in\mathcal{H}}$  and  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$  computes  $(\mathbf{W}^j)_{j\in\mathcal{C}}$  by evaluating their respective circuits, the same way they were computed in the ideal world. Then the parties secret share  $(\mathbf{W}^1,\ldots,\mathbf{W}^n)$  using slicing.

Fourth, the parties compute a sharing  $\{\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{mixed}}\}$  by evaluating  $H^{\mathsf{mixed}}$  on their shares of the inputs  $([z], [z'], \{\mathbf{z}\}, \{\mathbf{z}'\})$ . Each party j already holds shares  $[z]_j, [z']_j$  and values  $z_j, z'_j$ . Then the parties locally secret share  $\mathbf{z}, \mathbf{z}'$  using slicing. For each  $j \in [n]$ , they compute:

$$\{z_j\} = \operatorname{slice}(z_j, 1, j)$$
  
 $\{z'_j\} = \operatorname{slice}(z'_j, 1, j)$ 

and set

$$\{\mathbf{z}\} = (\{z_1\}, \dots, \{z_n\})$$
  
 $\{\mathbf{z}'\} = (\{z_1'\}, \dots, \{z_n'\})$ 

 $\{\mathbf{z}\}, \{\mathbf{z}'\}$  are valid Shamir sharings of degree n-1. At this point, the parties hold valid sharings  $[z], [z'], \{\mathbf{z}\}, \{\mathbf{z}'\}$ . Finally,  $H^{\mathsf{mixed}}$  applies a linear function to  $z, z', \mathbf{z}, \mathbf{z}'$ . If the parties apply  $H^{\mathsf{mixed}}$  to their shares  $[z], [z'], \{\mathbf{z}\}, \{\mathbf{z}'\}$ , then the wire values that they compute will represent a valid Shamir sharing of  $\mathbf{W}^{\mathsf{mixed}}$ . This is because Shamir sharing is linearly homomorphic.

In summary, given the real-world sharing  $\{\mathbf{W}\}$ , SSS.open $(\mathbb{F}, \{\mathbf{W}\})$  has the same distribution as the ideal-world  $\mathbf{W}$ .

**Operation ZK:** Let us consider one execution of op = ZK in the ideal world and compare it to the real-world execution of  $\pi_{ZK}$ .

<span id="page-65-0"></span>**Lemma 41.** Given an execution of op = ZK, if the correspondence property (Definition 33) is satisfied before the execution, then it will still be satisfied after the execution.

<span id="page-65-1"></span>Proof. The proof is similar to the one for  $\mathsf{op} = \mathsf{Increment}$  (Lemmas 39 and 40). ZK computes an instance-witness pair  $\mathsf{inst} = (\overline{E}, u, \overline{W}, \mathsf{x})$  and  $\mathsf{w} = (\mathbf{E}, r_{\mathbf{E}}, \mathbf{W}, r_{\mathbf{W}})$ , and we must show that the distribution of (inst, w), given the adversary's view, is the same in the real and ideal worlds. We can use the argument from Lemma 39 to show that  $\mathsf{inst} = (\overline{E}, u, \overline{W}, \mathsf{x})$  and  $(\mathbf{E}, r_{\mathbf{E}}, r_{\mathbf{W}})$  have the same distribution in the real and ideal worlds. It just remains to show that  $\mathsf{W}$  has the same distribution in the real and ideal worlds. This is proven in Lemma 42

{66}------------------------------------------------

**Lemma 42.** Given an execution of op = ZK, the real-world protocol computes shares  $\{\mathbf{W}\}$ , and the ideal-world functionality computes  $\mathbf{W}$  such that  $SSS.open(\mathbb{F}, \{\mathbf{W}\})$  and  $\mathbf{W}$  are identically distributed given the adversary's view.

*Proof.* This proof is similar to that of Lemma 40.

In the ideal world, ZK (Fig. 4.8) computes W to be all the input and intermediate wire values of

$$H_F(\mathsf{vk}_\mathsf{NIFS},\mathsf{mode},\mathsf{mode}',\ell,z_0,z,\vec{\omega},\mathsf{inst}_\perp,\mathsf{Inst},\mathsf{inst}',\pi,\pi',\mathbf{h},\mathbf{z},\mathbf{z}',\mathbf{r},\mathbf{r}')$$

Here is how the functionality chooses the inputs to  $H_F$ .  $(\ell, z_0, \mathsf{Inst}, \mathsf{inst}', \pi, \pi')$  are provided as inputs to  $\mathcal{S}$ .  $[z_0]_{\mathcal{C}}$  is obtained when  $\mathcal{S}$ 's simulation of  $\mathcal{A}$  provides it as input to  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{input}}$ .  $\mathbf{r}'_{\mathcal{C}}$  is chosen by  $\mathcal{S}$ 's simulation of  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$ . The functionality sets  $(\mathsf{mode}', \vec{\omega}) = (\mathsf{zk}, \mathbf{0})$ . The functionality computes  $(\mathsf{vk}_{\mathsf{NIFS}}, \mathsf{inst}_{\perp})$  from  $\mathsf{pk}_{\mathsf{NIFS}}$ , which was given to the functionality during initialization. If  $\ell \geq 1$ , then  $(\mathsf{mode}, z, \mathbf{z}, \mathbf{h}, \mathbf{r})$  are read from the state variables as follows:

$$(\mathsf{mode}, z, \mathbf{z}, \mathbf{h}, \mathbf{r}) \leftarrow (\mathsf{inst}_B, \mathsf{w}_B).(\mathsf{mode}', z', \mathbf{z}', \mathbf{h}', \mathbf{r}')$$

If  $\ell = 0$ , then (mode,  $z, \mathbf{z}, \mathbf{h}, \mathbf{r}$ ) are computed from the inputs  $(z_0, [z_0]_{\mathcal{C}})$  as follows:

$$(\mathsf{mode}, z, \mathbf{z}, \mathbf{h}, \mathbf{r}) = (\mathsf{increment}, z_0, \mathsf{SSS}.\mathsf{Share}(\mathbb{F}, z_0, [z_0]_{\mathcal{C}}, \mathcal{C}, t), \mathbf{0}^{n+1}, \mathbf{0}^n)$$

Next, the functionality sets  $\mathbf{z}' = \mathbf{z}$ . Finally the functionality samples  $\mathbf{r}'_{\mathcal{H}}$  uniformly at random.

In the real world (Section 5.1.4),  $\mathcal{H}$ ,  $\mathcal{A}$ , and  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$  compute the inputs of  $H_F$ , and the inputs have the same distribution as they do in the ideal world. ( $\ell$ ,  $z_0$ , Inst, inst, inst',  $\pi$ ,  $\pi'$ ) are provided as inputs to  $\mathcal{H}$  and  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$ .  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$  obtains  $[z_0]_{\mathcal{C}}$  when  $\mathcal{A}$  provides it as input to  $\mathcal{F}_{input}$ .  $\mathbf{r}'_{\mathcal{C}}$  is chosen by  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$ .  $\mathcal{H}$  and  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$  choose (mode',  $\vec{\omega}$ ) = (zk, 0).  $\mathcal{H}$  and  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$  compute (vk<sub>NIFS</sub>, inst<sub>\(\perp}\)) from pk<sub>NIFS</sub>, which was given to them during initialization. If  $\ell \geq 1$ , then (mode, [z], z, h, r) are read from the state variables as follows:</sub>

$$(\mathsf{mode},[z],\mathbf{z},\mathbf{h},\mathbf{r}) \leftarrow (\mathsf{inst}_B,[\mathsf{w}_B]).(\mathsf{mode}',\mathbf{z}',\mathbf{z}',\mathbf{h}',\mathbf{r}')$$

Note that  $[z] = [w_B].\mathbf{z}'$ , and in the ideal world,  $z = w_B.z'$ . This ensures that SSS.open( $\mathbb{F}, [z]$ ) equals the ideal-world value of z because  $[w_B].\mathbf{z}'$  represents a valid sharing of  $w_B.z'$ .

If  $\ell = 0$ , then (mode,  $[z], \mathbf{z}, \mathbf{h}, \mathbf{r}$ ) are computed from the inputs  $(z_0, [z_0]_{\mathcal{C}})$  as follows:

$$(\mathsf{mode}, \mathbf{z}, \mathbf{h}, \mathbf{r}) = (\mathsf{increment}, \mathsf{SSS}.\mathsf{Share}(\mathbb{F}, z_0, [z_0]_{\mathcal{C}}, \mathcal{C}, t), \mathbf{0}^{n+1}, \mathbf{0}^n)$$
$$[z] = \mathbf{z}$$

In this case, SSS.open( $\mathbb{F}$ , [z]) =  $z_0$ , which is the ideal-world value of z.

Next, the parties set  $\mathbf{z}' = \mathbf{z}$ . Finally  $\mathcal{H}$  samples  $\mathbf{r}'_{\mathcal{H}}$  uniformly at random. In summary, we have listed all the inputs to  $H_F$  and shown that they have the same distribution in the real and ideal worlds.

Next, the real-world parties compute a valid sharing of the wire values  $\{\mathbf{W}\}$  of the circuit  $H_F$  evaluated on the inputs above. The proof of this follows the same argument as the one in Lemma 40. Then SSS.open( $\mathbb{F}, \{\mathbf{W}\}$ ) has the same distribution as the ideal-world  $\mathbf{W}$ .

## Simulating the Adversary's View During Output

Now that we have proven the correspondence property (see Definition 33 and Lemma 34), we can prove that S correctly simulates the adversary's view of the Output operation, which reconstructs a subset of the running state (inst<sub>A</sub>, w<sub>A</sub>, inst<sub>B</sub>, w<sub>B</sub>, inst<sub>C</sub>, w<sub>C</sub>). First, the values that the parties reconstruct have the same distribution in the real and ideal worlds because of the correspondence property. Second, the reconstruction procedure does not reveal any information other than the reconstructed values. This is because the parties re-randomize their shares of [w<sub>A</sub>], [w<sub>B</sub>], or [w<sub>C</sub>] by adding a random sharing {0}, before publishing their shares. We will make this argument formal in Lemma 43.

<span id="page-66-0"></span>**Lemma 43.** After an execution of op = Output, the adversary's view is the same in the real and ideal worlds.

*Proof.* It suffices to consider the first time that op = Output because after this operation, the functionality stops responding.

{67}------------------------------------------------

At the start of the first invocation of Output, the joint distribution of  $(inst_A, w_A, inst_B, w_B, inst_C, w_C)$  and the adversary's view is the same in the real and ideal worlds. First, Lemma 32 shows that the adversary's view at the start of Output is identically distributed in the real and ideal worlds. Second, Lemma 34 shows that the distribution of  $(inst_A, w_A, inst_B, w_B, inst_C, w_C)$ , given the adversary's view, is the same in the real and ideal worlds. Therefore the joint distribution of  $(inst_A, w_A, inst_B, w_B, inst_C, w_C)$  and the adversary's view is the same in the real and ideal worlds. Let us fix values for the running state  $(inst_A, w_A, inst_B, w_B, inst_C, w_C)$  and the adversary's view.

Next, let us consider the real world protocol for Output (Section 5.1.6), for each position  $P \in L$  that will be outputted. First,  $\mathcal{A}$  sends to  $\mathcal{F}_{input}$  its shares  $\{\mathbf{0}\}_{\mathcal{C}}$ , and  $\mathcal{H}$  receives from  $\mathcal{F}_{input}$  the remaining shares  $\{\mathbf{0}\}_{\mathcal{H}}$ . Then  $\mathcal{H}$  and  $\mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{SH}}$  compute:

$$[w_P]' = [w_P] + \{0\}$$

The shares  $[w_P]_{\mathcal{C}}^{\mathsf{SH}}$  are uniquely determined by  $[w_P]_{\mathcal{C}}^{\mathsf{SH}}$  and  $\{\mathbf{0}\}_{\mathcal{C}}^{\mathsf{SH}}$ , which are determined by the adversary's view. But even given the adversary's view,  $[w_P]_{\mathcal{H}}'$  are uniformly random, due to the randomness of  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathrm{input}}$ , over all sharings such that:

$$\mathbf{w}_{P} = SSS.\mathsf{open}\left(\mathbb{F}, ([\mathbf{w}_{P}]'_{\mathcal{H}}, [\mathbf{w}_{P}]'_{\mathcal{C}}^{\mathsf{SH}})\right) \tag{5.4}$$

<span id="page-67-0"></span>Next, in the ideal world S samples  $[w_P]'_{\mathcal{H}}$  from the real-world distribution. S samples  $[w_P]'_{\mathcal{H}}$  uniformly at random from all shares that satisfy Eq. (5.4). Therefore, the shares  $[w_P]'_{\mathcal{H}}$  given to  $\mathcal{A}$  are identically distributed in the real and ideal worlds.  $[w_P]'_{\mathcal{H}}$  is the only messsage that  $\mathcal{A}$  receives during  $\pi_{\text{Output}}$ , so the adversary's view at the end of Output is identically distributed in the real and ideal worlds.

## Simulating the Honest Parties' Outputs

We have already shown that the adversary's view is correctly simulated. It just remains to show that the joint distribution of the adversary's view and the honest parties' outputs is correctly simulated. This true because many of the honest parties' outputs are actually determined by the adversary's view. We make this argument formal in Lemma 44.

<span id="page-67-1"></span>**Lemma 44.** At the end of the protocol, the joint distribution of the adversary's view and the honest parties' outputs is the same in the real and ideal worlds.

*Proof.* Lemma 43 shows that at the end of the protocol, the adversary's view is the same in the real and ideal worlds. Next, let us consider all the honest party outputs for each operation  $op \in \{Input, Fold, Increment, ZK, Rand, Output\}$  and show that these outputs, conditioned on the adversary's view, have the same distribution in the real and ideal worlds.

When op = Input: The honest parties don't have any outputs in the real or ideal world.

When  $op \in \{Increment, ZK\}$ : The honest parties output inst', which is the value of  $inst_B$  at the end of the operation. The correspondence property (Definition 33 and Lemma 34) implies that  $inst_B$ , conditioned on the adversary's view, is identically distributed in the real and ideal worlds. Therefore, the distribution of the honest parties' output, conditioned on the adversary's view, has the same distribution in the real and ideal worlds.

When op = Rand: The honest parties output inst', which is the value of  $inst_C$  at the end of the operation. Once again, the correspondence property implies that this output, conditioned on the adversary's view, has the same distribution in the real and ideal worlds.

When op = Fold: The honest parties output (inst<sub>A</sub>,  $\pi'$ ).

The correspondence property implies that the distribution of  $inst_A$ , conditioned on the adversary's view, has the same distribution in the real and ideal worlds.

Now let us consider  $\pi'$ . In the real world,  $\pi'$  is the value  $\overline{T}$  that the parties reconstruct in  $\pi_{\mathsf{Fold}}$  (Section 5.1.2):

$$\pi' = \mathsf{SSS.open}(\mathbb{G}, \{\overline{T}\})$$

{68}------------------------------------------------

In the ideal world, S simulates the reconstruction of  $\pi'$ , and the value that S reconstructs is the value of  $\pi'$  that the honest parties output. If the adversary is malicious, then S sends  $\pi'$  to the functionality, which sends this value to the honest parties. If the adversary is semi-honest, then S makes sure that the value of  $\pi'$  that is reconstructed in its simulation matches the value  $\pi$  that the functionality will send to the honest parties.

Since S correctly simulates the adversary's view, and  $\pi'$  is part of the adversary's view in the real and ideal worlds, that implies that the distribution of  $\pi'$  is the same in the real and ideal worlds.

When op = Output: The honest parties output (inst'<sub>P</sub>, w'<sub>P</sub>) for each  $P \in L$ .

In the real world,  $\operatorname{inst}_P' = \operatorname{inst}_P$ , which the parties previously computed, and  $w_P'$  is the witness that the parties reconstruct in  $\pi_{\operatorname{Output}}$  (Section 5.1.6).

In the ideal world,  $\mathcal{S}$  chooses  $\mathsf{inst}_P' = \mathsf{inst}_P$ , which was already stored in the functionality's state. Then  $\mathsf{w}_P'$  is the witness that  $\mathcal{A}$  reconstructs in  $\mathcal{S}$ 's simulation. Both of these outputs are determined by the adversary's view, so they have the same distribution in the real and ideal worlds.

{69}------------------------------------------------
